The purpose of this document is to provide a complete, technically rigorous, and reproducible record of the final implemented state of the lab environment, the access-control model selected for the environment, the operational validation performed against that environment, and the incident simulation used to demonstrate practical NAC troubleshooting and remediation workflows.

This document is intentionally written as an engineering implementation and validation report rather than as a tutorial, learning journal, or informal case study. Where the lab departs from production architecture due to virtualization or platform constraints, those departures are stated explicitly and evaluated in context rather than implied or omitted.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 1.3: INTENDED AUDIENCE AND USAGE
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection defines who the document is for, how it should be read,
% and what interpretive assumptions apply when reviewing the lab.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{1.3 Intended Audience and Usage}
\label{sec:intended-audience-and-usage}

This document is intended for the following audiences:

\begin{itemize}
    \item Network engineering practitioners evaluating the design and validation of a virtualized NAC proof-of-concept;
    \item Security engineering practitioners reviewing the use of wired IEEE 802.1X, RADIUS-backed authentication, and segmented guest policy enforcement;
    \item Systems engineering practitioners assessing service integration across routing, firewalling, authentication, logging, ITSM, and automation domains;
    \item Technical reviewers evaluating the project as a portfolio artifact intended to demonstrate enterprise-relevant design and operational execution rather than isolated tool familiarity.
\end{itemize}

This document should be interpreted as a formal record of the final implemented state of the environment and the authoritative incident validation path, not as a chronological build log. Intermediate troubleshooting states, temporary misconfigurations, and transient construction artifacts that were used only to arrive at the final design are intentionally excluded unless they are directly relevant to the incident narrative or to explaining an architectural decision.

The operational evidence included in this document is constrained by the following rules:

\begin{itemize}
    \item Final-state command outputs, configuration files, service states, validation results, and automation artifacts are considered in scope;
    \item Packet capture interpretation and protocol-state observations are considered in scope;
    \item Full debug logs are not reproduced wholesale; only explicitly selected excerpts are included where necessary to support an incident conclusion;
    \item GUI-derived evidence (e.g., pfSense, GLPI, Wireshark) is represented by screenshots rather than terminal transcription unless a manual transcription is operationally clearer;
    \item Sensitive values, including passwords, shared secrets, tokens, and authentication material, MUST be redacted in all published forms of the document.
\end{itemize}

The environment documented herein is intentionally designed as a constrained but defensible approximation of enterprise NAC behavior within a virtualization platform. In particular, the guest-access model implemented in this lab is a policy-based guest access simulation enforced through Linux and pfSense control points rather than true switch-driven dynamic VLAN reassignment. This distinction is central to the technical integrity of the document and will be treated explicitly in subsequent sections.

% =====================================================================
% SECTION 2: EXECUTIVE SUMMARY
% =====================================================================
% This section provides a concise but formal summary of the lab's
% purpose, final outcome, major design decisions, and operational value.
% It is intentionally written in enterprise engineering language rather
% than in tutorial or retrospective style.
% =====================================================================

\section{2. Executive Summary}
\label{sec:executive-summary}

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 2.1: OBJECTIVE
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection states the primary objective of the lab in formal
% engineering terms.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{2.1 Objective}
\label{sec:objective}

The objective of this implementation was to design, build, and validate a small-scale but enterprise-relevant Network Access Control (NAC) proof-of-concept within a fully virtualized lab environment. The target outcome was not merely to demonstrate isolated component installation, but to establish an integrated control path in which a wired endpoint could be authenticated via IEEE 802.1X against a RADIUS policy authority, granted or denied access based on authentication state, observed through centralized logging and evidence collection, and incorporated into a realistic incident-response workflow.

The implemented design intentionally spans multiple operational domains in order to reflect the way NAC is encountered in practice. These domains include endpoint authentication, routed segmentation, firewall policy enforcement, service observability, IT service management (ITSM) workflow, automation-assisted validation, and packet-level protocol analysis. The lab was therefore scoped to produce not only a functional access-control environment, but also a defensible record of how that environment behaves under both normal and failure conditions.

The specific technical objectives of the lab were as follows:

\begin{itemize}
    \item Implement a wired IEEE 802.1X authentication path using a Linux-based authenticator simulation and a FreeRADIUS policy server;
    \item Establish authenticated access to routed service resources for a validated endpoint;
    \item Implement a constrained guest-access model for unauthenticated or otherwise non-trusted endpoints using policy-based enforcement at the firewall boundary;
    \item Integrate local observability through service logs, structured incident evidence files, and Splunk Universal Forwarder monitoring inputs;
    \item Integrate ITSM workflow through GLPI by recording the incident as an actual tracked ticket associated with a managed asset;
    \item Implement meaningful automation through a Python evidence parser and an Ansible validation/remediation workflow;
    \item Validate both failure and recovery states using terminal evidence, service logs, and packet captures reviewed in Wireshark.
\end{itemize}

The result is intended to function as a technically rigorous proof-of-concept demonstrating that a virtualized environment can be used to model core NAC control flows and operational procedures, provided that platform limitations are explicitly acknowledged and the chosen enforcement model is documented accurately.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 2.2: OUTCOME SUMMARY
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection summarizes the final implemented outcome and the major
% validation conclusions at a high level.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{2.2 Outcome Summary}
\label{sec:outcome-summary}

The lab successfully achieved its core design and validation objectives.

A functioning wired IEEE 802.1X authentication path was implemented using a Linux-based access node acting as a wired authenticator, a Linux endpoint acting as the supplicant, and FreeRADIUS as the central authentication and policy service. The authenticated endpoint path was validated through successful RADIUS test transactions, successful supplicant authorization during controlled test execution, and confirmed routed reachability to service-network resources once the authorization path was operating as intended.

Because the environment was built entirely from virtual machines and not from managed enterprise switching hardware, the design could not support true switch-driven dynamic VLAN reassignment in the same manner as a production NAC deployment. Rather than obscuring this limitation, the implementation explicitly adopted a policy-based guest access simulation. In this model, unauthenticated or non-trusted endpoint behavior was represented by placement in a dedicated guest network segment behind pfSense, where DHCP, gateway access, and tightly constrained reachability were permitted while access to protected service resources was denied. This design choice preserved the operational intent of differentiated access control while remaining technically honest about the constraints of the platform.

The lab further succeeded in demonstrating an operational incident lifecycle rather than only a steady-state build. A wired 802.1X authentication failure was deliberately introduced in a controlled manner, evidence of the failed state was captured across the supplicant, authenticator, and RADIUS service layers, and the resulting condition was documented as a real incident in GLPI. Remediation was then performed by restoring the known-good client configuration and executing a clean re-test, after which successful authorization indicators were observed and the incident was closed as resolved.

Automation was incorporated in a manner that is materially relevant to the environment rather than ornamental. A Python script was developed to parse and summarize incident evidence artifacts, providing a deterministic summary of failed and remediated states. An Ansible playbook was developed to perform repeatable validation of NAC-related services and to support a bounded, operator-invoked remediation mode. These automation artifacts reinforce the document's central claim that the lab demonstrates not only component familiarity, but also repeatable operational procedure.

Finally, packet capture analysis was added as a first-class validation layer. Using \texttt{tcpdump} on the authenticator host and subsequent inspection in Wireshark, the lab captured both failed and successful authentication flows across the EAPOL and RADIUS control paths. This provided protocol-level corroboration of the incident narrative and strengthened the evidentiary value of the environment as a portfolio artifact.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 2.3: ARCHITECTURAL CONSTRAINTS AND DESIGN DECISION
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection states the key architectural constraint and the
% resulting design decision in clear terms so that the rest of the
% document can rely on that framing without ambiguity.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{2.3 Architectural Constraints and Design Decision}
\label{sec:architectural-constraints-and-design-decision}

The most important architectural constraint in this lab is that the environment does not include a managed access switch capable of acting as a production-grade IEEE 802.1X authenticator with RADIUS-driven dynamic VLAN reassignment, downloadable ACLs, or hardware-enforced post-auth policy state. Instead, the environment uses Ubuntu Server virtual machines and VMware virtual networks to model the control plane and segmentation boundaries.

This constraint has direct implications. While Linux user-space tools such as \texttt{hostapd} and \texttt{wpa\_supplicant} can accurately model important aspects of wired IEEE 802.1X authentication and EAP state transitions, they do not convert a general-purpose virtual machine into a fully equivalent enterprise access switch. As a result, the environment can credibly demonstrate:

\begin{itemize}
    \item supplicant-to-authenticator EAPOL exchanges;
    \item authenticator-to-RADIUS request handling;
    \item successful and failed authentication states;
    \item policy differentiation between trusted and guest access paths;
    \item operational validation, logging, and incident response workflows.
\end{itemize}

However, it cannot credibly claim to implement:

\begin{itemize}
    \item true dynamic VLAN reassignment on a switchport as the direct result of RADIUS policy;
    \item hardware-native port reauthorization behavior;
    \item switch-specific enforcement semantics such as vendor ACL or downloadable ACL features.
\end{itemize}

For that reason, the guest-access component of this lab is explicitly defined as a policy-based guest access simulation rather than as a true dynamic guest VLAN implementation. The enforcement boundary is achieved through Linux and pfSense control points: the access path models authentication state, while pfSense enforces the differentiated reachability policy for the guest segment. This is not mutually exclusive with the broader segmentation model used throughout the lab; rather, it is the most defensible way to preserve the operational objective of differentiated access within the constraints of a virtualized platform.

This design decision is central to the technical integrity of the document. It allows the environment to remain enterprise-relevant without overstating equivalence to production hardware behavior, and it ensures that the incident and automation workflows presented later in the document are evaluated against the architecture that was actually built, not against an idealized architecture that was not.

% =====================================================================
% SECTION 3: SCOPE, OBJECTIVES, AND DESIGN ASSUMPTIONS
% =====================================================================
% This section formally defines what is and is not included in the lab,
% the assumptions under which the design should be interpreted, and the
% criteria by which the implementation is considered successful.
% =====================================================================

\section{3. Scope, Objectives, and Design Assumptions}
\label{sec:scope-objectives-and-design-assumptions}

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 3.1: IN-SCOPE COMPONENTS
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection enumerates the technologies and functional domains that
% are intentionally included in the implementation.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{3.1 In-Scope Components}
\label{sec:in-scope-components}

The following components and functional domains are in scope for this implementation and are treated as authoritative elements of the final environment:

\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Virtualized routing and segmentation:} multi-segment VMware virtual networking, Linux routing on intermediate nodes, and pfSense routing/firewall policy between guest and service segments;
    \item \textbf{Wired IEEE 802.1X authentication path:} a Linux-based wired authenticator simulation using \texttt{hostapd}, a Linux supplicant using \texttt{wpa\_supplicant}, and FreeRADIUS as the authentication and policy service;
    \item \textbf{RADIUS policy service:} FreeRADIUS user and client definitions, service validation, local and remote authentication testing, and runtime logging;
    \item \textbf{Guest access control model:} policy-based guest access simulation enforced by pfSense rather than true dynamic VLAN reassignment;
    \item \textbf{Observability and evidence collection:} service logs, journald evidence, explicit incident capture files, and packet capture collection with \texttt{tcpdump};
    \item \textbf{Log monitoring instrumentation:} Splunk Universal Forwarder configured to monitor relevant system and FreeRADIUS log sources;
    \item \textbf{ITSM workflow integration:} GLPI deployment, managed asset creation, incident ticket creation, incident update, and incident closure;
    \item \textbf{Automation artifacts:} Python-based incident evidence parser and Ansible-based validation/remediation workflow;
    \item \textbf{Packet-level protocol validation:} failed and successful EAPOL and RADIUS captures reviewed in Wireshark and interpreted as part of the incident narrative.
\end{itemize}

The lab is therefore intentionally broader than a single-service build. It is designed to demonstrate cross-domain operational competence across networking, security, Linux services, observability, automation, and incident handling.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 3.2: OUT-OF-SCOPE COMPONENTS
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection states what the implementation does not claim to be and
% what production-grade features are intentionally excluded.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{3.2 Out-of-Scope Components}
\label{sec:out-of-scope-components}

The following capabilities, platforms, or production-grade behaviors are explicitly out of scope for this implementation:

\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Managed enterprise switching hardware} with native switchport control semantics, hardware-enforced post-auth state, or vendor-specific NAC integration features;
    \item \textbf{True dynamic VLAN reassignment} directly applied to an access port by RADIUS attributes during live port authorization;
    \item \textbf{Downloadable ACLs (dACLs)} or equivalent vendor-specific post-auth policy enforcement constructs;
    \item \textbf{Certificate-based enterprise EAP methods} such as EAP-TLS; the lab uses a simpler method appropriate for deterministic proof-of-concept validation rather than a full PKI-backed endpoint identity model;
    \item \textbf{Full Splunk Enterprise deployment} including indexer, search head, dashboards, or distributed search architecture; the lab intentionally uses Splunk Universal Forwarder only;
    \item \textbf{High availability or clustering} for core services such as RADIUS, firewalling, or ITSM;
    \item \textbf{Production secrets management} such as Vault-backed secret retrieval, certificate lifecycle automation, or centrally governed credential rotation;
    \item \textbf{Full configuration management pipeline integration} such as CI/CD, GitOps enforcement, or scheduled compliance scans beyond the bounded automation included here.
\end{itemize}

These exclusions do not weaken the document; rather, they define the limits within which the implementation should be evaluated. The lab is intended to be a credible and technically honest proof-of-concept, not a claim of feature parity with a production NAC deployment.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 3.3: DESIGN ASSUMPTIONS
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection captures the assumptions under which the architecture
% and validation logic should be interpreted.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{3.3 Design Assumptions}
\label{sec:design-assumptions}

The architecture and validation logic in this document rely on the following design assumptions:

\begin{itemize}
    \item VMware virtual networks are treated as deterministic Layer 2 broadcast domains suitable for representing isolated access, transit, service, and guest segments;
    \item Ubuntu Server virtual machines can credibly model routing, firewall-adjacent policy boundaries, service hosting, and user-space authenticator/supplicant behavior, but not the full control semantics of a managed enterprise switch;
    \item The Linux-based access node provides a valid proof-of-concept wired authenticator role for demonstrating EAPOL state transitions and RADIUS interaction, even though it is not a substitute for hardware switch behavior;
    \item pfSense provides the authoritative guest-policy enforcement boundary in the final design, including guest subnet gatewaying, DHCP, and restrictive firewall policy;
    \item Temporary host-side management IP assignments on VMware virtual network interfaces are acceptable as an operational convenience for accessing isolated management surfaces during build and validation, provided those assignments are clearly described and not misrepresented as part of the guest or authenticated endpoint data path;
    \item Final-state validation evidence is more relevant to the purpose of this document than full chronological build history, and therefore only final-state and incident-relevant evidence is included;
    \item Packet capture interpretation is treated as corroborating evidence for the control-plane narrative rather than as the sole source of truth; service state and incident logs remain authoritative in combination with capture findings.
\end{itemize}

These assumptions are intentionally conservative. They preserve the lab's credibility by clearly separating what is being simulated from what is being directly implemented.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 3.4: SUCCESS CRITERIA
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection defines the objective conditions under which the lab is
% considered successful and portfolio-worthy.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{3.4 Success Criteria}
\label{sec:success-criteria}

The implementation is considered successful only if all of the following criteria are satisfied:

\begin{itemize}
    \item A wired endpoint can successfully complete the intended 802.1X authentication flow through the Linux-based authenticator and FreeRADIUS service under controlled test conditions;
    \item The authenticated path provides the expected routed reachability to protected service-network resources in the final-state topology;
    \item A guest or otherwise non-trusted endpoint path can be demonstrated as operationally distinct, including guest DHCP and restricted reachability enforced by pfSense policy;
    \item FreeRADIUS can be validated both locally and remotely using deterministic test transactions and final-state service health checks;
    \item Splunk Universal Forwarder is configured to monitor the intended log sources relevant to NAC and incident evidence collection;
    \item GLPI is operational and used to represent a real incident record associated with a managed asset, rather than being present only as an unused supporting service;
    \item A controlled wired 802.1X authentication failure can be reproduced, observed, and documented across the supplicant, authenticator, and RADIUS layers;
    \item The failed condition can be remediated, and a clean re-test can confirm restoration of successful authorization;
    \item The incident can be summarized through automation using the Python evidence parser and validated through repeatable service checks using the Ansible workflow;
    \item Packet captures of both failed and successful authentication flows can be collected and interpreted in a way that supports the incident narrative and final validation conclusions.
\end{itemize}

The standard applied throughout this document is whether the environment can support a coherent access-control narrative, a reproducible incident, and a technically defensible remediation and validation process.

% =====================================================================
% SECTION 4: REFERENCE ARCHITECTURE
% =====================================================================
% This section defines the authoritative logical architecture of the lab
% in its final implemented state. It is intentionally written as a clean
% architectural description rather than as a chronological build record.
% Supporting raw configuration artifacts and final-state command outputs
% are referenced forward to the appendices where applicable.
% =====================================================================

\section{4. Reference Architecture}
\label{sec:reference-architecture}

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 4.1: LOGICAL TOPOLOGY OVERVIEW
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection describes the major systems, trust boundaries, and
% traffic paths in the final architecture.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{4.1 Logical Topology Overview}
\label{sec:logical-topology-overview}

The final lab environment is implemented as a multi-segment virtualized network hosted on VMware Workstation Pro running on an Ubuntu 24.04 workstation. The design intentionally separates management, authenticated access, routed transit, service hosting, and guest access into distinct logical domains in order to model the control boundaries that would exist in a small enterprise environment.

At a high level, the architecture consists of the following functional layers:

\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Endpoint access layer:} a Linux endpoint (\texttt{client-1}) acting as the wired supplicant and a Linux access node (\texttt{access-node}) acting as the wired IEEE 802.1X authenticator using \texttt{hostapd};
    \item \textbf{Routed access transit:} a Linux distribution router (\texttt{dist-router}) providing the intermediate routed path between the access domain and the firewall-controlled service domain;
    \item \textbf{Security and policy boundary:} a pfSense virtual firewall providing routing, interface separation, guest DHCP, and guest-policy enforcement between protected services and guest access;
    \item \textbf{Protected service domain:} a services segment containing \texttt{radius-splunk} (FreeRADIUS, Splunk Universal Forwarder, Python/Ansible automation) and \texttt{ops-glpi} (GLPI, Apache, MariaDB);
    \item \textbf{Guest access domain:} a separate pfSense-controlled guest network used to simulate unauthenticated or non-trusted endpoint behavior through policy-based access restriction rather than dynamic switch-driven VLAN reassignment;
    \item \textbf{Out-of-band management paths:} VMware-provided host-side virtual network access used where necessary for administrative reachability to otherwise isolated interfaces during build and validation.
\end{itemize}

The most important architectural distinction in this environment is the separation between authentication state and guest-policy enforcement state. Authentication is modeled directly in the Linux-based 802.1X control path between the supplicant, the authenticator, and FreeRADIUS. Guest-policy enforcement is modeled at the pfSense boundary, where a dedicated guest subnet receives DHCP service and is restricted from reaching protected service resources. This division is intentional and reflects the platform constraint that the lab cannot perform true switch-native dynamic VLAN reassignment.

In practical terms, the environment therefore demonstrates the following control-plane relationships:

\begin{itemize}
    \item \texttt{client-1} can authenticate through \texttt{access-node} to FreeRADIUS when operating in the wired 802.1X validation path;
    \item \texttt{access-node} forwards RADIUS authentication traffic toward the protected services segment via \texttt{dist-router} and pfSense routing;
    \item \texttt{radius-splunk} provides the policy authority for the authentication workflow and also serves as the automation control node;
    \item \texttt{ops-glpi} provides the ITSM platform used to represent the incident as an actual tracked operational record;
    \item pfSense enforces the guest access model by providing DHCP and tightly scoped reachability on the guest segment while preventing direct access from the guest segment to protected services.
\end{itemize}

This architecture is sufficiently complex to demonstrate realistic operational dependencies without becoming so large that the evidentiary chain becomes difficult to follow. The final design is therefore intentionally narrow in breadth but deep in validation.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 4.2: NETWORK SEGMENTATION AND ADDRESSING
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection defines the authoritative subnet and interface model
% used throughout the document. Supporting final-state command outputs
% and configuration files will be referenced in appendices.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{4.2 Network Segmentation and Addressing}
\label{sec:network-segmentation-and-addressing}

The final environment uses multiple RFC1918 segments to model distinct functional domains. These segments are intentionally small and purpose-driven. The authoritative logical addressing model used throughout this document is summarized below.

\textbf{Logical Network Segments and Addressing}

\medskip

\textbf{Access Client Segment} \\
\textbf{Subnet:} \texttt{10.0.12.0/24} \\
\textbf{Primary Function:} Authenticated endpoint attachment segment for the wired supplicant validation path. \\
\textbf{Representative Systems / Interfaces:} \texttt{client-1} (\texttt{10.0.12.10}), \texttt{access-node} access-facing interface (\texttt{10.0.12.1}).

\medskip

\textbf{Access-to-Distribution Transit} \\
\textbf{Subnet:} \texttt{10.0.23.0/24} \\
\textbf{Primary Function:} Routed transit between the access node and the intermediate distribution router. \\
\textbf{Representative Systems / Interfaces:} \texttt{access-node} (\texttt{10.0.23.1}), \texttt{dist-router} (\texttt{10.0.23.2}).

\medskip

\textbf{Protected Services Segment} \\
\textbf{Subnet:} \texttt{10.0.30.0/24} \\
\textbf{Primary Function:} Service hosting for RADIUS, logging, automation control, and ITSM systems. \\
\textbf{Representative Systems / Interfaces:} pfSense OPT1 (\texttt{10.0.30.1}), \texttt{radius-splunk} (\texttt{10.0.30.10}), \texttt{ops-glpi} (\texttt{10.0.30.20}).

\medskip

\textbf{Distribution-to-Firewall Transit} \\
\textbf{Subnet:} \texttt{10.0.34.0/30} \\
\textbf{Primary Function:} Point-to-point transit between the Linux distribution router and pfSense. \\
\textbf{Representative Systems / Interfaces:} \texttt{dist-router} (\texttt{10.0.34.1}), pfSense LAN/transit (\texttt{10.0.34.2}).

\medskip

\textbf{Guest Access Segment} \\
\textbf{Subnet:} \texttt{10.0.20.0/24} \\
\textbf{Primary Function:} Guest or non-trusted endpoint segment used for policy-based guest access simulation. \\
\textbf{Representative Systems / Interfaces:} pfSense GUEST/OPT2 (\texttt{10.0.20.254}), DHCP pool (\texttt{10.0.20.100--10.0.20.199}).

\medskip

\textbf{Host / Hypervisor Management Segments} \\
\textbf{Subnet:} VMware-provided RFC1918 segments (e.g., \texttt{192.168.195.0/24}, \texttt{192.168.205.0/24}). \\
\textbf{Primary Function:} Administrative reachability and host-level VM management paths. \\
\textbf{Representative Systems / Interfaces:} Host virtual interfaces, VM management adapters, temporary host-side management access where required.

\medskip

The protected services segment (\texttt{10.0.30.0/24}) is the most operationally significant network in the final design. It hosts the RADIUS policy authority, the log-forwarding instrumentation, the Python and Ansible automation artifacts, and the ITSM platform used to record the incident lifecycle. This segment is reachable from the authenticated access path through the routed chain \texttt{access-node} $\rightarrow$ \texttt{dist-router} $\rightarrow$ pfSense, and it is intentionally protected from direct guest-segment access by pfSense firewall policy.

The guest access segment (\texttt{10.0.20.0/24}) is not intended to be interpreted as a dynamically assigned VLAN in the switch-native sense. Rather, it is the authoritative guest-policy enforcement domain used by this lab to represent how an unauthenticated or otherwise restricted endpoint would be treated under a constrained virtualized implementation. The guest segment receives DHCP from pfSense and can reach the guest gateway for local validation, but it is intentionally restricted from reaching the protected services segment as part of the policy model described later in Section~\ref{sec:access-control-model-and-policy-enforcement}.

The authoritative final-state interface configurations and route outputs that support this addressing model will be captured later in the document and in the appendices. Where exact file-based network definitions are relevant, they will be cross-referenced directly (for example, see Section~\ref{sec:appendix-a-radius-splunk-netplan-config}, Section~\ref{sec:appendix-a-ops-glpi-netplan-config}, Section~\ref{sec:appendix-a-access-node-netplan-config}, Section~\ref{sec:appendix-a-client-1-netplan-config}, and Section~\ref{sec:platform-build-and-final-state-configuration}).

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 4.3: FINAL SYSTEM ROLES
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection defines the authoritative role of each major node in
% the final design and creates forward references for appendices.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{4.3 Final System Roles}
\label{sec:final-system-roles}

Each system in the final lab has a deliberately constrained but clearly defined role. This section establishes those roles so that later configuration, validation, and incident sections can refer to each node without ambiguity.

\subsubsection{4.3.1 access-node}
\label{sec:access-node-role}

\texttt{access-node} is the primary access-layer control point in the lab. It serves as the Linux-based wired IEEE 802.1X authenticator using \texttt{hostapd} in wired mode and also functions as the first routed hop away from the endpoint access segment. Its responsibilities include:

\begin{itemize}
    \item providing the access-facing interface for the wired supplicant path;
    \item originating EAPOL interaction with the supplicant during controlled 802.1X testing;
    \item forwarding RADIUS authentication requests toward the FreeRADIUS service;
    \item maintaining static routing toward the protected services domain via \texttt{dist-router};
    \item serving as the packet capture location for both EAPOL and RADIUS control-plane captures during incident validation.
\end{itemize}

Its final-state configuration artifacts will be documented later (see Section~\ref{sec:appendix-a-access-node-netplan-config}, Section~\ref{sec:appendix-a-access-node-hostapd-wired-config}, Section~\ref{sec:appendix-a-access-node-hostapd-wired-systemd-unit}, and Section~\ref{sec:platform-build-and-final-state-configuration}).

\subsubsection{4.3.2 client-1}
\label{sec:client-1-role}

\texttt{client-1} is the endpoint used to represent a wired supplicant. It participates in both the authenticated access validation path and the guest-policy simulation path, depending on the test being executed. Its responsibilities include:

\begin{itemize}
    \item acting as the 802.1X supplicant using \texttt{wpa\_supplicant} in wired mode;
    \item serving as the primary subject of the simulated authentication failure and remediation workflow;
    \item demonstrating guest-segment behavior when attached to the pfSense-controlled guest network;
    \item providing endpoint-perspective evidence for successful and failed authorization states.
\end{itemize}

Its final-state network configuration and wired supplicant configuration will be documented later (see Section~\ref{sec:appendix-a-client-1-netplan-config}, Section~\ref{sec:appendix-a-client-1-wpa-supplicant-wired-8021x-config}, and Section~\ref{sec:appendix-b-incident-evidence-files}).

\subsubsection{4.3.3 dist-router}
\label{sec:dist-router-role}

\texttt{dist-router} is the intermediate Linux routing node that provides the transit path between the access-layer control domain and the pfSense-protected service domain. It exists to make the architecture more representative of a routed enterprise topology rather than collapsing all functions into a single flat segment. Its responsibilities include:

\begin{itemize}
    \item routing traffic between the access-node transit segment and the pfSense transit interface;
    \item providing the next hop from the access domain toward the protected services segment;
    \item supporting final-state path validation and demonstrating that the RADIUS control path traverses a realistic routed intermediate node.
\end{itemize}

Its final-state interface and route evidence will be included later as part of the validation and appendix materials (see Section~\ref{sec:platform-build-and-final-state-configuration}).

\subsubsection{4.3.4 pfSense}
\label{sec:pfsense-role}

pfSense is the authoritative security boundary and policy-enforcement node in the final design. It separates the protected services domain from the guest domain and provides the guest gateway, guest DHCP service, and restrictive guest firewall policy. Its responsibilities include:

\begin{itemize}
    \item terminating the routed transit from \texttt{dist-router};
    \item providing the protected services segment gateway;
    \item providing the guest segment gateway;
    \item issuing DHCP leases on the guest segment;
    \item enforcing the guest access policy that differentiates guest reachability from authenticated service reachability;
    \item serving as the primary firewall boundary used to simulate guest access control in the absence of true switch-native dynamic VLAN reassignment.
\end{itemize}

Its final interface assignments, DHCP configuration, and guest firewall rules will be documented later (see Section~\ref{sec:appendix-a-pfsense-authenticated-interface-summary}, Section~\ref{sec:appendix-a-pfsense-guest-interface-summary}, Section~\ref{sec:appendix-a-pfsense-guest-firewall-rules}, and the related screenshots in the main body).

\subsubsection{4.3.5 radius-splunk}
\label{sec:radius-splunk-role}

\texttt{radius-splunk} is the most functionally dense system in the lab and serves as both the protected authentication authority and the automation/observability control node. It hosts:

\begin{itemize}
    \item FreeRADIUS as the central authentication and policy service;
    \item Splunk Universal Forwarder configured to monitor relevant local log sources;
    \item the Python incident evidence parser used to summarize failed and remediated states;
    \item the Ansible control environment used for repeatable validation and bounded remediation workflows.
\end{itemize}

Because this node combines policy authority, observability, and automation, it is central to both the steady-state validation path and the incident-response narrative. Its configuration artifacts will be referenced extensively later (see Section~\ref{sec:appendix-a-radius-splunk-netplan-config}, Section~\ref{sec:appendix-a-radius-splunk-freeradius-users-config}, Section~\ref{sec:appendix-a-radius-splunk-freeradius-clients-config}, Section~\ref{sec:appendix-a-splunk-uf-inputs-conf}, Section~\ref{sec:appendix-a-python-nac-incident-parser}, and Section~\ref{sec:appendix-a-ansible-nac-validation-playbook}).

\subsubsection{4.3.6 ops-glpi}
\label{sec:ops-glpi-role}

\texttt{ops-glpi} provides the IT service management layer for the lab. It is intentionally not treated as a passive supporting service; instead, it is used to represent the incident as an actual tracked operational record tied to a managed asset. Its responsibilities include:

\begin{itemize}
    \item hosting GLPI on Apache with MariaDB backend services;
    \item maintaining the \texttt{client-1} asset record used in the incident workflow;
    \item maintaining the incident ticket used to document the failed authentication event, remediation, and closure.
\end{itemize}

Its final-state configuration and deployment validation will be documented later (see Section~\ref{sec:appendix-a-ops-glpi-netplan-config} and Section~\ref{sec:glpi-final-configuration}).

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

% =====================================================================
% SECTION 5: PLATFORM BUILD AND FINAL-STATE CONFIGURATION
% =====================================================================
% This section documents the final implemented state of the major systems
% in the lab. It is intentionally limited to authoritative final-state
% configuration and service posture. Intermediate troubleshooting states
% are excluded unless later required by the incident narrative.
% =====================================================================

\section{5. Platform Build and Final-State Configuration}
\label{sec:platform-build-and-final-state-configuration}

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 5.1: HYPERVISOR AND VIRTUAL NETWORK LAYOUT
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection describes the virtualization substrate and the role of
% VMware virtual networks in the architecture.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{5.1 Hypervisor and Virtual Network Layout}
\label{sec:hypervisor-and-virtual-network-layout}

The lab is hosted on VMware Workstation Pro running on an Ubuntu 24.04 workstation. Distinct VMware virtual networks are used to model access, transit, service, guest, and management domains. These virtual networks are treated as deterministic Layer 2 broadcast domains and are mapped into the final architecture as functional segments rather than as ad hoc connectivity conveniences.

In the final design, the principal virtual network roles are as follows:

\begin{itemize}
    \item a management/NAT network used for host-side administrative reachability to selected virtual machines;
    \item an access segment for the wired endpoint path;
    \item a transit segment between the access node and the intermediate router;
    \item a protected services segment behind pfSense;
    \item a guest segment behind pfSense used for the policy-based guest access simulation.
\end{itemize}

The actual VMware GUI configuration is not reproduced here because it does not materially improve the engineering value of the document. Instead, the document records the final interface-to-segment mapping in operational terms through the final netplan files, interface summaries, and pfSense interface assignments. This is more appropriate for a production-style technical record and avoids overemphasizing hypervisor UI state over the logical architecture.

Temporary host-side IP assignments on VMware host interfaces were used at specific points to reach otherwise isolated pfSense interfaces during setup and validation. Those temporary host assignments are not treated as part of the access-control data path and are not part of the final guest or authenticated endpoint model.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 5.2: ROUTER AND FIREWALL ROLES
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection establishes the final-state role of dist-router and
% pfSense as the intermediate routing and policy-enforcement nodes.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{5.2 Router and Firewall Roles}
\label{sec:router-and-firewall-roles}

The routing and policy boundary in the final design is split across two nodes:

\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{\texttt{dist-router}} provides the routed transition from the access domain toward the pfSense transit interface;
    \item \textbf{pfSense} provides the protected services gateway, the guest gateway, guest DHCP, and the firewall enforcement boundary.
\end{itemize}

This separation is important because it makes the architecture more representative of a routed enterprise environment. Rather than allowing the access node to connect directly to the service segment, the design forces service-bound traffic through an intermediate routed hop and then through the firewall boundary before it reaches the protected service network.

The final-state interface and route posture on \texttt{dist-router} confirms this role. The node maintains:

\begin{itemize}
    \item a management/NAT-connected interface for administrative access;
    \item a transit-facing interface on \texttt{10.0.23.0/24} toward \texttt{access-node};
    \item a point-to-point transit interface on \texttt{10.0.34.0/30} toward pfSense;
    \item a static route to the access client segment via \texttt{10.0.23.1};
    \item a static route to the protected services segment via pfSense at \texttt{10.0.34.2}.
\end{itemize}

The final-state interface summary and routing table for \texttt{dist-router} are reproduced below.

\begin{verbatim}
dist-router: ip -br a
lo               UNKNOWN        127.0.0.1/8 ::1/128
ens33            UP             192.168.195.142/24 metric 100 fe80::20c:29ff:fe4f:9ae7/64
ens38            UP             10.0.23.2/24 fe80::20c:29ff:fe4f:9af1/64
ens39            UP             10.0.34.1/30 fe80::20c:29ff:fe4f:9afb/64
\end{verbatim}

\begin{verbatim}
dist-router: ip route
default via 192.168.195.2 dev ens33 proto dhcp src 192.168.195.142 metric 100
10.0.12.0/24 via 10.0.23.1 dev ens38 proto static
10.0.23.0/24 dev ens38 proto kernel scope link src 10.0.23.2
10.0.30.0/24 via 10.0.34.2 dev ens39 proto static
10.0.34.0/30 dev ens39 proto kernel scope link src 10.0.34.1
192.168.195.0/24 dev ens33 proto kernel scope link src 192.168.195.142 metric 100
192.168.195.2 dev ens33 proto dhcp scope link src 192.168.195.142 metric 100
\end{verbatim}

\begin{verbatim}
dist-router: sysctl net.ipv4.ip_forward
net.ipv4.ip_forward = 1
\end{verbatim}

The corresponding final netplan configuration on \texttt{dist-router} is reproduced in full below and is also referenced in Appendix A (see Section~\ref{sec:appendix-a-dist-router-netplan-config}).

\begin{verbatim}
dist-router: /etc/netplan/50-cloud-init.yaml
network:
  version: 2
  ethernets:
    ens33:
      dhcp4: true
    ens38:
      dhcp4: false
      addresses:
        - 10.0.23.2/24
      routes:
        - to: 10.0.12.0/24
          via: 10.0.23.1
    ens39:
      dhcp4: false
      addresses:
        - 10.0.34.1/30
      routes:
        - to: 10.0.30.0/24
          via: 10.0.34.2
\end{verbatim}

pfSense is the authoritative security boundary in the environment. Its final interface structure is as follows:

\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{WAN} (\texttt{em0}) on the VMware management/NAT network at \texttt{192.168.195.145/24};
    \item \textbf{LAN / transit} (\texttt{em1}) at \texttt{10.0.34.2/30};
    \item \textbf{OPT1 / services} (\texttt{em2}) at \texttt{10.0.30.1/24};
    \item \textbf{GUEST / OPT2} (\texttt{em3}) at \texttt{10.0.20.254/24}.
\end{itemize}

This interface design allows pfSense to sit directly between the routed access path and the protected services segment, while also hosting a guest segment whose policy can be enforced independently.

The authoritative manual transcription of the final pfSense interface summary is reproduced below and in the appendix (see Section~\ref{sec:appendix-a-pfsense-authenticated-interface-summary}).

\begin{verbatim}
pfSense final interface summary
WAN (wan) -> em0 -> v4/DHCP4: 192.168.195.145/24
LAN (lan) -> em1 -> v4: 10.0.34.2/30
OPT1 (opt1) -> em2 -> v4: 10.0.30.1/24
GUEST (opt2) -> em3 -> v4: 10.0.20.254/24
\end{verbatim}

The associated pfSense interfaces terminal screenshot is shown below:

\begin{figure}[H]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{assets/screenshots/pfSense_Interfaces.png}
    \caption{pfSense interface assignments showing WAN, LAN/transit, OPT1 (services), and GUEST (OPT2)}
    \label{sec:fig-pfsense_interfaces}
\end{figure}

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 5.3: FREERADIUS FINAL CONFIGURATION
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection records the final-state RADIUS service posture,
% relevant file excerpts, and local validation.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{5.3 FreeRADIUS Final Configuration}
\label{sec:freeradius-final-configuration}

FreeRADIUS is hosted on \texttt{radius-splunk} and functions as the authoritative authentication and policy service for the wired 802.1X workflow. In the final design, FreeRADIUS accepts requests from the Linux-based authenticator on \texttt{access-node}, validates the known endpoint credential, and provides the Access-Accept or reject path used throughout the validation and incident workflows.

The final-state network posture of \texttt{radius-splunk} is reproduced below.

\begin{verbatim}
radius-splunk: ip -br a
lo               UNKNOWN        127.0.0.1/8 ::1/128
ens33            UP             192.168.195.143/24 metric 100 fe80::20c:29ff:fe82:9c22/64
ens38            UP             10.0.30.10/24 fe80::20c:29ff:fe82:9c2c/64
\end{verbatim}

\begin{verbatim}
radius-splunk: ip route
default via 192.168.195.2 dev ens33 proto dhcp src 192.168.195.143 metric 100
10.0.12.0/24 via 10.0.30.1 dev ens38 proto static
10.0.23.0/24 via 10.0.30.1 dev ens38 proto static
10.0.30.0/24 dev ens38 proto kernel scope link src 10.0.30.10
10.0.34.0/30 via 10.0.30.1 dev ens38 proto static
192.168.195.0/24 dev ens33 proto kernel scope link src 192.168.195.143 metric 100
192.168.195.2 dev ens33 proto dhcp scope link src 192.168.195.143 metric 100
\end{verbatim}

\begin{verbatim}
radius-splunk: /etc/netplan/50-cloud-init.yaml
network:
  version: 2
  ethernets:
    ens33:
      dhcp4: true
    ens38:
      dhcp4: false
      addresses:
        - 10.0.30.10/24
      routes:
        - to: 10.0.34.0/30
          via: 10.0.30.1
        - to: 10.0.23.0/24
          via: 10.0.30.1
        - to: 10.0.12.0/24
          via: 10.0.30.1
\end{verbatim}

The FreeRADIUS service is enabled and active in the final state. Its service status confirms a successful pre-start configuration check and a normal running state.

\begin{verbatim}
radius-splunk: systemctl status freeradius --no-pager
Loaded: loaded (/usr/lib/systemd/system/freeradius.service; enabled; preset: enabled)
Active: active (running) since Tue 2026-03-24 04:24:32 UTC
Main PID: 1162 (freeradius)
Status: "Processing requests"

Recent lines:
Compiling Post-Auth-Type Client-Lost for attr Post-Auth-Type
radiusd: #### Skipping IP addresses and Ports ####
Configuration appears to be OK
Started freeradius.service - FreeRADIUS multi-protocol policy server.
\end{verbatim}

The explicit configuration validation command was also executed successfully in the final state.

\begin{verbatim}
radius-splunk: sudo freeradius -XC
FreeRADIUS Version 3.2.5
Starting - reading configuration files ...
Configuration appears to be OK
\end{verbatim}

The local authorization source contains the lab user entry required for the controlled 802.1X workflow. Only the relevant excerpt is reproduced below, with the shared credential redacted. The full authoritative excerpt is referenced later in Appendix A (see Section~\ref{sec:appendix-a-radius-splunk-freeradius-users-config}).

\begin{verbatim}
radius-splunk: grep excerpt from /etc/freeradius/3.0/mods-config/files/authorize
205-#########################################################
206-
207:nacuser Cleartext-Password := "<REDACTED>"
\end{verbatim}

The RADIUS client definition for the Linux-based authenticator is reproduced below, again with the shared secret redacted. The authoritative appendix target for this excerpt is Section~\ref{sec:appendix-a-radius-splunk-freeradius-clients-config}.

\begin{verbatim}
radius-splunk: grep excerpt from /etc/freeradius/3.0/clients.conf
354:client access-node {
355-    ipaddr = 10.0.23.1
356-    secret = <REDACTED>
357:    shortname = access-node
358-}
\end{verbatim}

A deterministic local RADIUS test using \texttt{radtest} confirms that the configured lab user is accepted by the running service. The command line has been redacted where necessary, but the full output is reproduced below.

\begin{verbatim}
radius-splunk: local radtest
radtest nacuser '<REDACTED_PASSWORD>' 127.0.0.1 0 '<REDACTED_SHARED_SECRET>'
Sent Access-Request Id 54 from 0.0.0.0:45969 to 127.0.0.1:1812 length 77
        User-Name = "nacuser"
        User-Password = "<REDACTED>"
        NAS-IP-Address = 127.0.1.1
        NAS-Port = 0
        Message-Authenticator = 0x00
        Cleartext-Password = "<REDACTED>"
Received Access-Accept Id 54 from 127.0.0.1:1812 to 127.0.0.1:45969 length 38
        Message-Authenticator = 0xe9369309c447d3d395f2e7af45be62b5
\end{verbatim}

Taken together, these artifacts establish that the final FreeRADIUS posture is internally consistent, locally testable, and suitable for the later remote validation and incident scenario sections.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 5.4: HOSTAPD WIRED AUTHENTICATOR FINAL CONFIGURATION
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection records the final hostapd configuration and managed
% service posture on access-node.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{5.4 hostapd Wired Authenticator Final Configuration}
\label{sec:hostapd-wired-authenticator-final-configuration}

The wired authenticator role is implemented on \texttt{access-node} using \texttt{hostapd} in wired mode. This is one of the central design decisions in the lab because it allows the environment to model the IEEE 802.1X control path in a Linux-based virtualized setting while remaining explicit that this does not provide full parity with managed enterprise switch behavior.

The final interface posture of \texttt{access-node} is reproduced below.

\begin{verbatim}
access-node: ip -br a
lo               UNKNOWN        127.0.0.1/8 ::1/128
ens33            UP             192.168.195.141/24 metric 100 fe80::20c:29ff:feef:5e82/64
ens38            UP             10.0.12.1/24 fe80::20c:29ff:feef:5e8c/64
ens39            UP             10.0.23.1/24 fe80::20c:29ff:feef:5e96/64
\end{verbatim}

\begin{verbatim}
access-node: ip route
default via 192.168.195.2 dev ens33 proto dhcp src 192.168.195.141 metric 100
10.0.12.0/24 dev ens38 proto kernel scope link src 10.0.12.1
10.0.23.0/24 dev ens39 proto kernel scope link src 10.0.23.1
10.0.30.0/24 via 10.0.23.2 dev ens39 proto static
192.168.195.0/24 dev ens33 proto kernel scope link src 192.168.195.141 metric 100
192.168.195.2 dev ens33 proto dhcp scope link src 192.168.195.141 metric 100
\end{verbatim}

\begin{verbatim}
access-node: /etc/netplan/50-cloud-init.yaml
network:
  version: 2
  ethernets:
    ens33:
      dhcp4: true
    ens38:
      dhcp4: false
      addresses:
        - 10.0.12.1/24
    ens39:
      dhcp4: false
      addresses:
        - 10.0.23.1/24
      routes:
        - to: 10.0.30.0/24
          via: 10.0.23.2
\end{verbatim}

\begin{verbatim}
access-node: sysctl net.ipv4.ip_forward
net.ipv4.ip_forward = 1
\end{verbatim}

The final \texttt{hostapd} wired configuration is reproduced below, with the shared secret redacted. The authoritative appendix target for this file is Section~\ref{sec:appendix-a-access-node-hostapd-wired-config}.

\begin{verbatim}
access-node: /etc/hostapd/lab/hostapd-wired.conf
interface=ens38
driver=wired

ieee8021x=1
eapol_version=2
eap_reauth_period=3600

auth_server_addr=10.0.30.10
auth_server_port=1812
auth_server_shared_secret=<REDACTED>

own_ip_addr=10.0.23.1

logger_stdout=-1
logger_stdout_level=0
logger_syslog=-1
logger_syslog_level=0

ctrl_interface=/var/run/hostapd
ctrl_interface_group=0
\end{verbatim}

To make the authenticator role operationally realistic and manageable, the lab does not rely solely on ad hoc foreground invocations. Instead, the final design includes a dedicated systemd unit, reproduced below and referenced later in Appendix A (see Section~\ref{sec:appendix-a-access-node-hostapd-wired-systemd-unit}).

\begin{verbatim}
access-node: /etc/systemd/system/hostapd-wired.service
[Unit]
Description=hostapd wired 802.1X authenticator (lab)
After=network-online.target
Wants=network-online.target

[Service]
Type=simple
ExecStart=/usr/sbin/hostapd -dd /etc/hostapd/lab/hostapd-wired.conf
Restart=on-failure
RestartSec=2

[Install]
WantedBy=multi-user.target
\end{verbatim}

The final service posture confirms that the unit is enabled and running, and that it is bound to the intended interface and RADIUS server.

\begin{verbatim}
access-node: systemctl status hostapd-wired.service --no-pager
Loaded: loaded (/etc/systemd/system/hostapd-wired.service; enabled; preset: enabled)
Active: active (running) since Tue 2026-03-24 04:20:41 UTC
Main PID: 975 (hostapd)

Recent lines:
Using interface ens38 with hwaddr <REDACTED> and ssid ""
ens38: RADIUS Authentication server 10.0.30.10:1812
RADIUS local address: 10.0.23.1:45833
ens38: interface state UNINITIALIZED->ENABLED
ens38: AP-ENABLED
ens38: Setup of interface done.
\end{verbatim}

A later section of this document will use the managed \texttt{hostapd-wired.service} unit as a core part of the Ansible validation and remediation workflow. Its existence therefore has architectural significance beyond convenience: it makes the authenticator role inspectable, restartable, and automatable in a way that is operationally meaningful.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 5.5: WPA_SUPPLICANT FINAL CONFIGURATION
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection records the endpoint-side supplicant configuration and
% clarifies the relationship between the running system service and the
% controlled test invocation model used later in the incident section.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{5.5 wpa\_supplicant Final Configuration}
\label{sec:wpa-supplicant-final-configuration}

The wired endpoint role is implemented on \texttt{client-1} using \texttt{wpa\_supplicant}. The final-state client network posture and the final controlled configuration file are reproduced below.

\begin{verbatim}
client-1: ip -br a
lo               UNKNOWN        127.0.0.1/8 ::1/128
ens33            UP             10.0.12.10/24 fe80::20c:29ff:fe68:5333/64
\end{verbatim}

\begin{verbatim}
client-1: ip route
default via 10.0.12.1 dev ens33 proto static
10.0.12.0/24 dev ens33 proto kernel scope link src 10.0.12.10
\end{verbatim}

\begin{verbatim}
client-1: /etc/netplan/50-cloud-init.yaml
network:
  version: 2
  ethernets:
    ens33:
      addresses:
        - 10.0.12.10/24
      routes:
        - to: default
          via: 10.0.12.1
\end{verbatim}

The final wired supplicant configuration is reproduced below, with the secret redacted. The authoritative appendix target for this file is Section~\ref{sec:appendix-a-client-1-wpa-supplicant-wired-8021x-config}.

\begin{verbatim}
client-1: /etc/wpa_supplicant/lab/wired-8021x.conf
ctrl_interface=/run/wpa_supplicant
ap_scan=0
eapol_version=2

network={
    key_mgmt=IEEE8021X
    eap=MD5
    identity="nacuser"
    password="<REDACTED>"
}
\end{verbatim}

In the final steady state at the time of evidence capture, the generic \texttt{wpa\_supplicant.service} unit is active:

\begin{verbatim}
client-1: systemctl status wpa_supplicant --no-pager
Loaded: loaded (/usr/lib/systemd/system/wpa_supplicant.service; enabled; preset: enabled)
Active: active (running) since Tue 2026-03-24 04:57:02 UTC
Main PID: 830 (wpa_supplicant)

Recent lines:
Starting wpa_supplicant.service - WPA supplicant...
Successfully initialized wpa_supplicant
Started wpa_supplicant.service - WPA supplicant.
\end{verbatim}

This final steady-state service condition should not be confused with the controlled test methodology used later in the incident section. During the failed-authentication simulation and subsequent remediation validation, the \texttt{wpa\_supplicant} service was deliberately stopped and the binary was invoked manually in debug mode against the lab-specific wired configuration file in order to obtain deterministic, isolated evidence. The final active service state reproduced here is therefore a valid representation of the system at rest, while the incident section will separately document the intentional service stop/start choices used during testing.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 5.6: SPLUNK UNIVERSAL FORWARDER FINAL CONFIGURATION
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection records the final UF posture and monitored inputs.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{5.6 Splunk Universal Forwarder Final Configuration}
\label{sec:splunk-universal-forwarder-final-configuration}

Splunk Universal Forwarder is installed on \texttt{radius-splunk} as a lightweight observability component. In this lab, it is not used as a full SIEM platform with local indexing and search-head analytics. Rather, it is used to demonstrate an enterprise-relevant log collection and monitoring posture for NAC-related evidence sources.

The final runtime state of the forwarder is reproduced below.

\begin{verbatim}
radius-splunk: /opt/splunkforwarder/bin/splunk status
Warning: Attempting to revert the SPLUNK_HOME ownership
Warning: Executing "chown -R root /opt/splunkforwarder"
splunkd is running (PID: 1671).
splunk helpers are running (PIDs: 1672).
\end{verbatim}

The monitored input definitions are maintained in a dedicated local app and are reproduced in full below. The authoritative appendix target for this file is Section~\ref{sec:appendix-a-splunk-uf-inputs-conf}.

\begin{verbatim}
radius-splunk: /opt/splunkforwarder/etc/apps/lab_nac_inputs/local/inputs.conf
[monitor:///var/log/syslog]
disabled = false
index = main
sourcetype = syslog

[monitor:///var/log/auth.log]
disabled = false
index = main
sourcetype = linux_secure

[monitor:///var/log/freeradius/radius.log]
disabled = false
index = main
sourcetype = freeradius
\end{verbatim}

These inputs are sufficient to demonstrate that the environment includes structured monitoring of the most relevant local evidence sources for the NAC scenario:

\begin{itemize}
    \item generic system events via \texttt{/var/log/syslog};
    \item authentication and privilege-related events via \texttt{/var/log/auth.log};
    \item FreeRADIUS service activity via \texttt{/var/log/freeradius/radius.log}.
\end{itemize}

This design choice is intentionally modest but meaningful. It allows the lab to claim concrete Splunk integration in a way that is accurate for the implemented scope, without overstating the environment as a full Splunk analytics deployment.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 5.7: GLPI FINAL CONFIGURATION
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection records the final-state web stack posture and the role
% of GLPI as an active ITSM component in the lab.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{5.7 GLPI Final Configuration}
\label{sec:glpi-final-configuration}

GLPI is hosted on \texttt{ops-glpi} and functions as the IT service management platform for the environment. It is not present only as a demonstration of software installation; it is used directly in the operational scenario to represent both the managed endpoint asset and the authentication incident ticket.

The final-state network posture of \texttt{ops-glpi} is reproduced below.

\begin{verbatim}
ops-glpi: ip -br a
lo               UNKNOWN        127.0.0.1/8 ::1/128
ens33            UP             192.168.195.144/24 metric 100 fe80::20c:29ff:fe46:c6a1/64
ens38            UP             10.0.30.20/24 fe80::20c:29ff:fe46:c6ab/64
\end{verbatim}

\begin{verbatim}
ops-glpi: ip route
default via 192.168.195.2 dev ens33 proto dhcp src 192.168.195.144 metric 100
10.0.12.0/24 via 10.0.30.1 dev ens38 proto static
10.0.23.0/24 via 10.0.30.1 dev ens38 proto static
10.0.30.0/24 dev ens38 proto kernel scope link src 10.0.30.20
10.0.34.0/30 via 10.0.30.1 dev ens38 proto static
192.168.195.0/24 dev ens33 proto kernel scope link src 192.168.195.144 metric 100
192.168.195.2 dev ens33 proto dhcp scope link src 192.168.195.144 metric 100
\end{verbatim}

\begin{verbatim}
ops-glpi: /etc/netplan/50-cloud-init.yaml
network:
  version: 2
  ethernets:
    ens33:
      dhcp4: true
    ens38:
      dhcp4: false
      addresses:
        - 10.0.30.20/24
      routes:
        - to: 10.0.34.0/30
          via: 10.0.30.1
        - to: 10.0.23.0/24
          via: 10.0.30.1
        - to: 10.0.12.0/24
          via: 10.0.30.1
\end{verbatim}

The Apache and MariaDB services are both enabled and active in the final state.

\begin{verbatim}
ops-glpi: systemctl status apache2 --no-pager

Active: active (running) since Tue 2026-03-24 05:22:11 UTC
Main PID: 1296 (apache2)

Recent lines:
Starting apache2.service - The Apache HTTP Server...
Started apache2.service - The Apache HTTP Server.
\end{verbatim}

\begin{verbatim}
ops-glpi: systemctl status mariadb --no-pager
Loaded: loaded (/usr/lib/systemd/system/mariadb.service; enabled; preset: enabled)

/usr/sbin/mariadbd: ready for connections.

\end{verbatim}

Local HTTP validation confirms that GLPI is exposed at the root path in the final Apache configuration, and that the historical \texttt{/glpi/} subpath is no longer the correct application path.

\begin{verbatim}

ops-glpi: curl -I http://127.0.0.1/glpi/
HTTP/1.1 404 Not Found
Date: Tue, 24 Mar 2026 05:28:14 GMT
Server: Apache/2.4.58 (Ubuntu)

Expires: Mon, 26 Jul 1997 05:00:00 GMT
Cache-Control: no-store, no-cache, must-revalidate
Pragma: no-cache
x-frame-options: SAMEORIGIN
Cache-Control: no-cache, private
Content-Type: text/html; charset=UTF-8
\end{verbatim}

The required PHP environment is present, including the previously installed \texttt{bcmath} extension required for GLPI.

\begin{verbatim}
ops-glpi: php -m | grep bcmath
bcmath
\end{verbatim}

Operationally, GLPI is not treated as a passive accessory to the environment. In the final state, it contains:

\begin{itemize}
    \item a managed asset record for \texttt{client-1};
    \item an incident ticket documenting the simulated wired 802.1X authentication failure and remediation workflow;
    \item a resolved ticket state reflecting successful remediation.
\end{itemize}

The associated GLPI GUI screenshots are shown below:

\begin{figure}[H]

\end{figure}

    \label{sec:fig-glpi_client-1_incident_ticket}
