% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 5.8: PYTHON AND ANSIBLE AUTOMATION ARTIFACTS
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection records the presence, role, and storage location of the
% automation artifacts in the final platform state. The full raw text of
% these artifacts is deferred to Appendix A for readability.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{5.8 Python and Ansible Automation Artifacts}
\label{sec:python-and-ansible-automation-artifacts}

The final platform state includes two automation artifacts that are operationally relevant to the lab:

\begin{itemize}
    \item a Python-based incident evidence parser hosted on \texttt{radius-splunk};
    \item an Ansible-based validation and optional remediation workflow, also hosted on \texttt{radius-splunk}.
\end{itemize}

These artifacts are treated as part of the implemented platform rather than as external supporting material because they are stored, executed, and maintained within the lab environment itself. Their purpose is not to inflate the apparent sophistication of the project, but to provide repeatable operational utility directly tied to the incident scenario described later in this document.

The Python parser is maintained under the local incident-evidence directory on \texttt{radius-splunk}. Its purpose is to consume the saved incident artifacts from the failed-authentication and successful-remediation workflows and produce a deterministic summary of whether the expected failure and recovery indicators are present. The authoritative full source of this parser is reproduced in Appendix A (see Section~\ref{sec:appendix-a-python-nac-incident-parser}).

The Ansible workflow is also maintained on \texttt{radius-splunk} and consists of:

\begin{itemize}
    \item an inventory file defining the local and remote NAC-related nodes;
    \item a validation and optional remediation playbook capable of checking service state, collecting recent logs, and conditionally restarting the core NAC services when explicitly requested by the operator.
\end{itemize}

The automation workflow uses SSH key-based authentication rather than hardcoded credentials, consistent with the document's stated security and publication posture. The authoritative full inventory and playbook contents are reproduced in Appendix A (see Section~\ref{sec:appendix-a-ansible-inventory-hosts-ini} and Section~\ref{sec:appendix-a-ansible-nac-validation-playbook}).

The presence of these artifacts in the final platform state is significant for two reasons. First, they demonstrate that the environment includes repeatable operational tooling rather than only static configuration. Second, they provide a bridge between the platform build described in this section and the incident-handling and validation workflows described later in Sections~\ref{sec:incident-scenario-wired-8021x-authentication-failure-and-remediation} and~\ref{sec:automation-workflows}.

% =====================================================================
% SECTION 6: ACCESS CONTROL MODEL AND POLICY ENFORCEMENT
% =====================================================================
% This section documents the final access-control model for the lab and
% validates that authenticated and guest traffic are handled according
% to the intended routed and firewall-enforced policy boundaries.
% =====================================================================

\section{6. Access Control Model and Policy Enforcement}
\label{sec:access-control-model-and-policy-enforcement}

The lab implements a deliberately segmented access model intended to resemble a simplified enterprise campus access design. Rather than treating the environment as a flat set of reachable subnets, the architecture distinguishes between authenticated endpoint access, protected services access, transit-only routing segments, and a separately governed guest segment.

This section documents the intended policy model and then validates that the environment behaves accordingly using direct path testing from both the authenticated endpoint and a dedicated guest validation node.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 6.1: POLICY MODEL OVERVIEW
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection defines the two principal trust domains in the lab,
% the expected behavior of each domain, and the internal networks from
% which the guest segment is intentionally isolated.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{6.1 Policy Model Overview}
\label{sec:policy-model-overview}

The final access-control model consists of two principal endpoint trust domains:

\begin{itemize}
    \item an \textbf{authenticated endpoint domain} represented by \texttt{client-1} on \texttt{10.0.12.0/24}, which is expected to reach protected services after successful wired 802.1X authentication;
    \item a \textbf{guest domain} represented by \texttt{guest-test-1} on \texttt{10.0.20.0/24}, which is expected to receive DHCP service and outbound internet access, but not internal access to protected or transit segments.
\end{itemize}

The routing and enforcement chain for authenticated access is:

\begin{center}
\texttt{client-1} $\rightarrow$ \texttt{access-node} $\rightarrow$ \texttt{dist-router} $\rightarrow$ \texttt{pfSense} $\rightarrow$ protected services
\end{center}

The guest enforcement chain is simpler:

\begin{center}
\texttt{guest-test-1} $\rightarrow$ \texttt{pfSense (GUEST)} $\rightarrow$ internet or explicitly permitted destinations
\end{center}

The protected services segment is represented by \texttt{10.0.30.0/24}, which hosts:

\begin{itemize}
    \item \texttt{radius-splunk} at \texttt{10.0.30.10};
    \item \texttt{ops-glpi} at \texttt{10.0.30.20}.
\end{itemize}

The guest segment is represented by \texttt{10.0.20.0/24} and is intentionally isolated from the following internal networks:

\begin{itemize}
    \item \texttt{10.0.30.0/24} (protected services);
    \item \texttt{10.0.34.0/30} (pfSense transit);
    \item \texttt{10.0.23.0/24} (access-to-distribution transit);
    \item \texttt{10.0.12.0/24} (authenticated endpoint/access segment).
\end{itemize}

This is not intended to model a full enterprise NAC enforcement platform with dynamic downloadable ACLs or per-session VLAN reassignment. Instead, it demonstrates a controlled and explainable policy boundary in which wired 802.1X authentication gates access into a routed path toward protected services, while a separate guest domain is explicitly restricted by firewall policy.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 6.2: AUTHENTICATED ENDPOINT ACCESS MODEL
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection documents the final-state posture of the authenticated
% endpoint and validates that it can reach the protected services path
% after successful wired 802.1X authentication.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{6.2 Authenticated Endpoint Access Model}
\label{sec:authenticated-endpoint-access-model}

The authenticated endpoint path is represented by \texttt{client-1}, which resides on \texttt{10.0.12.0/24} behind the Linux-based wired authenticator. In the final steady state, \texttt{client-1} uses a static address and default route via \texttt{access-node}.

The final-state endpoint interface and routing posture are reproduced below.

\begin{verbatim}
client-1: ip -br a
lo               UNKNOWN        127.0.0.1/8 ::1/128
ens33            UP             10.0.12.10/24 fe80::20c:29ff:fe68:5333/64
\end{verbatim}

\begin{verbatim}
client-1: ip route
default via 10.0.12.1 dev ens33 proto static
10.0.12.0/24 dev ens33 proto kernel scope link src 10.0.12.10
\end{verbatim}

The endpoint-side \texttt{wpa\_supplicant} service is active in the final steady state, which confirms that the endpoint is not merely configured but is operating in a post-build state consistent with the intended access model.

\begin{verbatim}
client-1: systemctl status wpa_supplicant --no-pager
Loaded: loaded (/usr/lib/systemd/system/wpa_supplicant.service; enabled; preset: enabled)
Active: active (running) since Tue 2026-03-24 08:03:28 UTC
Main PID: 829 (wpa_supplicant)

Recent lines:
Starting wpa_supplicant.service - WPA supplicant...
Started wpa_supplicant.service - WPA supplicant.
Successfully initialized wpa_supplicant
\end{verbatim}

From the endpoint perspective, successful access to the local gateway is verified first:

\begin{verbatim}
client-1: ping -c 4 10.0.12.1
4 packets transmitted, 4 received, 0% packet loss
\end{verbatim}

This confirms that the endpoint has Layer 3 reachability to the local routed access gateway on \texttt{access-node}. More importantly, the endpoint can also reach both protected services nodes on the \texttt{10.0.30.0/24} segment.

\begin{verbatim}
client-1: ping -c 4 10.0.30.10
4 packets transmitted, 4 received, 0% packet loss
\end{verbatim}

\begin{verbatim}
client-1: ping -c 4 10.0.30.20
4 packets transmitted, 4 received, 0% packet loss
\end{verbatim}

Application-layer validation to GLPI is also successful from the authenticated endpoint, which is stronger evidence than ICMP alone because it confirms that the routed path reaches a live service rather than merely a responsive host.

\begin{verbatim}
client-1: curl -I --max-time 5 http://10.0.30.20/
HTTP/1.1 200 OK
Date: Tue, 24 Mar 2026 08:21:04 GMT
Server: Apache/2.4.58 (Ubuntu)
Set-Cookie: <REDACTED>
Expires: Thu, 19 Nov 1981 08:52:00 GMT
Cache-Control: no-store, no-cache, must-revalidate
Pragma: no-cache
Cache-Control: no-cache, private
Vary: Accept-Encoding
Content-Type: text/html; charset=UTF-8
\end{verbatim}

This result is particularly useful because it shows that the authenticated endpoint can not only reach the protected services subnet but can directly access the GLPI web application on the services segment.

A \texttt{tracepath} test to \texttt{10.0.30.20} also supports the multi-hop design and shows that the path is not flat:

\begin{verbatim}
client-1: tracepath 10.0.30.20
 1?: [LOCALHOST]                      pmtu 1500
 1:  _gateway                                              0.911ms
 1:  _gateway                                              0.566ms
 2:  ???                                                   1.713ms
 3:  ???                                                   2.833ms
 4:  ???                                                   3.034ms reached
     Resume: pmtu 1500 hops 4 back 4
\end{verbatim}

Because intermediate Linux routers and pfSense do not necessarily reveal useful names or ICMP TTL-expired responses in a lab, the anonymous hop presentation is not treated as a defect. The important point is that the path is clearly multi-hop and terminates successfully at the protected service host.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 6.3: ROUTED PATH VALIDATION FOR AUTHENTICATED ACCESS
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection validates the authenticated endpoint path from the
% infrastructure perspective by showing the explicit forward and return
% routing behavior across the access, distribution, and firewall layers.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{6.3 Routed Path Validation for Authenticated Access}
\label{sec:routed-path-validation-for-authenticated-access}

The endpoint-side tests above establish that the authenticated client can reach the protected services segment. This subsection validates the same behavior from the infrastructure perspective to show that the path is explicitly routed and not the result of accidental adjacency.

On \texttt{access-node}, the route lookup for both protected service hosts confirms that traffic is forwarded to \texttt{dist-router} at \texttt{10.0.23.2} via interface \texttt{ens39}.

\begin{verbatim}
access-node: ip route get 10.0.30.10
10.0.30.10 via 10.0.23.2 dev ens39 src 10.0.23.1 uid 1000
\end{verbatim}

\begin{verbatim}
access-node: ip route get 10.0.30.20
10.0.30.20 via 10.0.23.2 dev ens39 src 10.0.23.1 uid 1000
\end{verbatim}

On \texttt{dist-router}, the route lookup confirms that both protected service hosts are then forwarded toward pfSense at \texttt{10.0.34.2} via interface \texttt{ens39}.

\begin{verbatim}
dist-router: ip route get 10.0.30.10
10.0.30.10 via 10.0.34.2 dev ens39 src 10.0.34.1 uid 1000
\end{verbatim}

\begin{verbatim}
dist-router: ip route get 10.0.30.20
10.0.30.20 via 10.0.34.2 dev ens39 src 10.0.34.1 uid 1000
\end{verbatim}

This proves the intended forward path:

\begin{center}
\texttt{client-1} $\rightarrow$ \texttt{access-node} $\rightarrow$ \texttt{dist-router} $\rightarrow$ \texttt{pfSense} $\rightarrow$ \texttt{10.0.30.0/24}
\end{center}

The return path is also explicitly validated from both protected service hosts. On \texttt{radius-splunk}, the route to the client subnet points to pfSense at \texttt{10.0.30.1}:

\begin{verbatim}
radius-splunk: ip route get 10.0.12.10
10.0.12.10 via 10.0.30.1 dev ens38 src 10.0.30.10 uid 1000
\end{verbatim}

On \texttt{ops-glpi}, the route to the client subnet is identical in principle:

\begin{verbatim}
ops-glpi: ip route get 10.0.12.10
10.0.12.10 via 10.0.30.1 dev ens38 src 10.0.30.20 uid 1000
\end{verbatim}

This confirms the intended return path:

\begin{center}
\texttt{10.0.30.0/24} $\rightarrow$ \texttt{pfSense} $\rightarrow$ \texttt{dist-router} $\rightarrow$ \texttt{access-node} $\rightarrow$ \texttt{client-1}
\end{center}

Bidirectional reachability is then directly validated with ICMP from both protected service hosts back to \texttt{client-1}.

\begin{verbatim}
radius-splunk: ping -c 4 10.0.12.10
4 packets transmitted, 4 received, 0% packet loss
\end{verbatim}

\begin{verbatim}
ops-glpi: ping -c 4 10.0.12.10
4 packets transmitted, 4 received, 0% packet loss
\end{verbatim}

This combination of forward-path route lookup, return-path route lookup, and bidirectional ICMP provides strong evidence that the authenticated endpoint path is functioning as a deliberately routed design rather than as an incidental lab shortcut.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 6.4: GUEST NETWORK POLICY MODEL
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection records the authoritative guest-segment DHCP and
% firewall policy on pfSense and defines the intended isolation model
% for the lower-trust guest network.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{6.4 Guest Network Policy Model}
\label{sec:guest-network-policy-model}

The guest network is implemented as a distinct pfSense-controlled segment on \texttt{10.0.20.0/24}. It is not intended to participate in the wired 802.1X access path and is instead used to demonstrate a separate, lower-trust network with DHCP service, firewall-enforced internal isolation, and outbound internet access.

The authoritative final pfSense interface definition for the guest segment was already established in Section~\ref{sec:router-and-firewall-roles}:

\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{GUEST / OPT2} (\texttt{em3}) at \texttt{10.0.20.254/24}
\end{itemize}

The final DHCP configuration for the guest segment is manually transcribed from pfSense as follows:

\begin{verbatim}
pfSense GUEST DHCP summary
Enabled (Enable DHCP server on GUEST interface)
Subnet: 10.0.20.0/24
Subnet Range: 10.0.20.1 - 10.0.20.254
Address Pool Range: 10.0.20.100 - 10.0.20.199
\end{verbatim}

The associated pfSense GUI screenshot is shown below:

\begin{figure}[H]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{assets/screenshots/pfSense_DHCP_Server_GUEST.png}
    \caption{pfSense GUEST DHCP server configuration for the policy-based guest access segment}
    \label{sec:fig-pfsense_dhcp_server_guest}
\end{figure}

The guest firewall policy is implemented as an ordered first-match ruleset on the pfSense GUEST interface. The final manually transcribed rules are reproduced below and were explicitly re-confirmed as unchanged and in the same top-to-bottom order at the time of Section 6 validation.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% NOTE: This ruleset is reproduced in full because rule order is central to
% the guest-policy interpretation and the top-to-bottom sequence is part
% of the technical meaning of the final pfSense configuration.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\begin{verbatim}
pfSense GUEST rules (top-to-bottom)
Pass  | IPv4* | Source: 10.0.20.0/24 | Destination: This Firewall (Self)
        | ALLOW-GUEST-TO-PFSENSE
Block | IPv4* | Source: 10.0.20.0/24 | Destination: 10.0.30.0/24       
        | BLOCK-GUEST-TO-SERVICES
Block | IPv4* | Source: 10.0.20.0/24 | Destination: 10.0.34.0/30       
        | BLOCK-GUEST-TO-TRANSIT
Block | IPv4* | Source: 10.0.20.0/24 | Destination: 10.0.23.0/24       
        | BLOCK-GUEST-TO-DIST
Block | IPv4* | Source: 10.0.20.0/24 | Destination: 10.0.12.0/24       
        | BLOCK-GUEST-TO-AUTH-ACCESS
Pass  | IPv4* | Source: 10.0.20.0/24 | Destination: *                  
        | ALLOW-GUEST-TO-INTERNET-AND-OTHER-NON-INTERNAL
\end{verbatim}

The associated pfSense GUI screenshot is shown below:

\begin{figure}[H]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{assets/screenshots/pfSense_Firewall_Rules_GUEST.png}
    \caption{pfSense GUEST interface firewall rules enforcing internal isolation and controlled outbound access}
    \label{sec:fig-pfsense_firewall_rules_guest}
\end{figure}

This ruleset is deliberately explicit. Rather than relying on an implicit deny plus a broad allow, the internal networks are individually enumerated and blocked before the final permissive rule. That makes the guest policy easier to explain, easier to audit, and more suitable for publication as a portfolio artifact.

At the time of validation, pfSense outbound NAT was confirmed to be in Automatic mode, which is relevant because it explains why the guest network can successfully access the internet without requiring separately documented manual NAT rules.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 6.5: GUEST DHCP AND ISOLATION VALIDATION
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection validates guest-segment behavior with a dedicated
% single-homed test VM by confirming DHCP lease assignment, gateway
% reachability, and enforced failure to reach protected internal paths.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{6.5 Guest DHCP and Isolation Validation}
\label{sec:guest-dhcp-and-isolation-validation}

To validate the guest policy empirically rather than only by configuration review, a dedicated disposable guest-only test VM named \texttt{guest-test-1} was created and attached solely to the pfSense GUEST segment. Importantly, this VM was provisioned with a single interface only and no management/NAT interface, so that its observed behavior reflects the actual guest policy boundary rather than an alternate administrative path.

The guest validation node received a DHCP lease from pfSense in the expected guest subnet and within the configured guest address pool.

\begin{verbatim}
guest-test-1: ip -br a
lo               UNKNOWN        127.0.0.1/8 ::1/128
ens33            UP             10.0.20.137/24 fe80::250:56ff:feab:cdef/64
\end{verbatim}

\begin{verbatim}
guest-test-1: ip route
default via 10.0.20.254 dev ens33 proto dhcp src 10.0.20.137 metric 100
10.0.20.0/24 dev ens33 proto kernel scope link src 10.0.20.137
10.0.20.254 dev ens33 proto dhcp scope link src 10.0.20.137 metric 100
\end{verbatim}

An explicit DHCP renewal confirms the expected pfSense DHCP behavior and the lease origin.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% NOTE: This DHCP renewal transcript is included because it provides direct
% lease-exchange evidence that pfSense is the active DHCP authority for
% the guest segment during validation.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\begin{verbatim}
guest-test-1: sudo dhclient -v ens33
DHCPDISCOVER on ens33 to 255.255.255.255 port 67 interval 3 (xid=0x4a3f2c1d)
DHCPDISCOVER on ens33 to 255.255.255.255 port 67 interval 6 (xid=0x4a3f2c1d)
DHCPOFFER of 10.0.20.137 from 10.0.20.254
DHCPREQUEST for 10.0.20.137 on ens33 to 255.255.255.255 port 67 (xid=0x4a3f2c1d)
DHCPACK of 10.0.20.137 from 10.0.20.254 (xid=0x4a3f2c1d)
bound to 10.0.20.137 -- renewal in 3600 seconds.
\end{verbatim}

The guest node can successfully reach the pfSense GUEST gateway itself, which is consistent with the first allow rule in the guest ruleset.

\begin{verbatim}
guest-test-1: ping -c 4 10.0.20.254
4 packets transmitted, 4 received, 0% packet loss
\end{verbatim}

The guest node is then tested against each explicitly blocked internal segment. All of these attempts fail as intended.

Protected services segment (\texttt{10.0.30.0/24}):

\begin{verbatim}
guest-test-1: ping -c 4 10.0.30.10
4 packets transmitted, 0 received, 100% packet loss
\end{verbatim}

\begin{verbatim}
guest-test-1: ping -c 4 10.0.30.20
4 packets transmitted, 0 received, 100% packet loss
\end{verbatim}

Transit segment toward pfSense (\texttt{10.0.34.0/30}):

\begin{verbatim}
guest-test-1: ping -c 4 10.0.34.1
4 packets transmitted, 0 received, 100% packet loss
\end{verbatim}

Access-to-distribution transit (\texttt{10.0.23.0/24}):

\begin{verbatim}
guest-test-1: ping -c 4 10.0.23.1
4 packets transmitted, 0 received, 100% packet loss
\end{verbatim}

Authenticated access segment (\texttt{10.0.12.0/24}):

\begin{verbatim}
guest-test-1: ping -c 4 10.0.12.1
4 packets transmitted, 0 received, 100% packet loss
\end{verbatim}

Application-layer access to GLPI from the guest node also fails by timeout, which is a particularly strong validation point because it demonstrates that the guest cannot reach a protected service even when directly attempting the published web application path.

\begin{verbatim}
guest-test-1: curl -I --max-time 5 http://10.0.30.20/
curl: (28) Connection timed out after 5001 milliseconds
\end{verbatim}

This confirms that the guest segment is not only blocked from protected hosts at the ICMP layer but is also effectively prevented from reaching the protected application service.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 6.6: GUEST INTERNET ACCESS VALIDATION
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection confirms that the guest network remains functional for
% general outbound connectivity and is not merely isolated from internal
% networks by broad denial.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{6.6 Guest Internet Access Validation}
\label{sec:guest-internet-access-validation}

Because the guest ruleset ends with an explicit allow rule for destinations outside the enumerated internal networks, it is useful to validate that the guest network retains general outbound access rather than being globally constrained.

The guest validation node can successfully reach a public IP address:

\begin{verbatim}
guest-test-1: ping -c 4 1.1.1.1
4 packets transmitted, 4 received, 0% packet loss
\end{verbatim}

It can also successfully retrieve HTTP headers from an external public site:

\begin{verbatim}
guest-test-1: curl -I --max-time 5 http://www.google.com
HTTP/1.1 200 OK
Content-Type: text/html; charset=ISO-8859-1
...
\end{verbatim}

This behavior is consistent with the intended guest policy model:

\begin{itemize}
    \item access to pfSense itself is permitted;
    \item access to enumerated internal networks is blocked;
    \item general outbound access is permitted.
\end{itemize}

In other words, the guest segment is not simply ``isolated'' in the abstract. It is specifically implemented as a constrained but functional internet-facing guest network.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 6.7: POLICY INTERPRETATION AND LIMITATIONS
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection summarizes the validated policy outcomes and states the
% architectural limits of the lab so that the access model is described
% accurately and not overstated.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{6.7 Policy Interpretation and Limitations}
\label{sec:policy-interpretation-and-limitations}

The validation results in this section support the following conclusions:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item The authenticated endpoint path is functional and routed as designed.
    \item The protected services segment is reachable from the authenticated endpoint and from no guest path tested.
    \item The guest network receives DHCP service correctly and is restricted from the enumerated internal networks.
    \item The guest network remains operational for general outbound internet access.
\end{enumerate}

This is a meaningful and defensible policy model for the scope of the lab. However, it is important to state its limits precisely.

First, the guest isolation is enforced by a conventional firewall ruleset on pfSense, not by dynamic NAC policy derived from endpoint identity or posture. That is acceptable for this design because the guest network is intentionally modeled as a separate trust zone rather than as a dynamically assigned 802.1X result.

Second, the authenticated endpoint path is validated at the network and application layers, but the environment does not implement per-session VLAN assignment, downloadable ACLs, posture checks, or certificate-based EAP methods. Those omissions are addressed later in the limitations section and do not invalidate the current demonstration.

Third, the Linux \texttt{hostapd} wired authenticator is used as a practical lab approximation of a switch-based 802.1X authenticator. It is appropriate for demonstrating the control-plane exchange and the operational workflow, but it should not be misrepresented as a drop-in replacement for enterprise access switch behavior.

Within those stated limits, the access-control model is internally consistent, technically validated, and appropriate for inclusion in a portfolio document that aims to present a realistic but accurately scoped virtualized NAC implementation.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 6.8: WIRESHARK CROSS-REFERENCE FOR POLICY VALIDATION
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection links the access-control conclusions in Section 6 to
% the packet-capture evidence reviewed later, noting that the policy
% model is also supported by observable 802.1X control-plane behavior.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{6.8 Wireshark Cross-Reference for Policy Validation}
\label{sec:wireshark-cross-reference-for-policy-validation}

Although the full packet-analysis narrative is reserved for the dedicated packet capture section later in this document, the Wireshark evidence already supports the access-control interpretation presented here.

At the time of review, the packet captures were confirmed to show that the authenticated endpoint traffic is preceded by EAPOL exchange rather than immediately presenting as ordinary post-authenticated data-plane traffic. This is important because it visually reinforces the intended distinction between:

\begin{itemize}
    \item the \textbf{pre-authentication control plane}, represented by EAPOL and RADIUS-driven 802.1X exchange; and
    \item the \textbf{post-authentication data plane}, represented by ordinary IP traffic after successful authorization.
\end{itemize}

The screenshot inventory includes all four relevant packet-capture perspectives:

\begin{itemize}
    \item failed client-side capture;
    \item failed authenticator-side capture;
    \item success client-side capture;
    \item success authenticator-side capture.
\end{itemize}

These artifacts will be incorporated in the dedicated packet-capture analysis section rather than duplicated here. Their relevance in this section is to reinforce that the access model is not inferred only from IP reachability tests; it is also observable at the protocol level through the expected 802.1X control-plane behavior.

% =====================================================================
% SECTION 7: INCIDENT SCENARIO -- WIRED 802.1X AUTHENTICATION FAILURE
%            AND REMEDIATION
% =====================================================================
% This section presents the central operational scenario of the lab: a
% controlled wired 802.1X authentication failure affecting client-1,
% followed by investigation, remediation, and successful recovery
% validation. The section is intentionally evidence-driven and ties
% together endpoint logs, authenticator behavior, RADIUS evidence, GLPI
% tracking, and the Python parser summary.
% =====================================================================

\section{7. Incident Scenario: Wired 802.1X Authentication Failure and Remediation}
\label{sec:incident-scenario-wired-8021x-authentication-failure-and-remediation}

The final lab environment is not presented solely as a static implementation. It is also used to simulate and document a realistic operational incident in which a wired endpoint fails 802.1X authentication, remains unauthorized, is investigated through multiple evidence sources, and is restored to service through a controlled remediation workflow.

This section documents that incident as the authoritative operational scenario for the environment. The incident validation date used throughout this section is 2026-03-22.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 7.1: INCIDENT DEFINITION
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection defines the simulated incident, identifies the control
% path involved, and establishes the operational goals of the failure-
% injection and recovery workflow.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{7.1 Incident Definition}
\label{sec:incident-definition}

The incident concerns the managed endpoint \texttt{client-1}, which is the same endpoint represented in GLPI and used throughout the access-control validation workflow. The endpoint normally authenticates through the following control path:

\begin{center}
\texttt{client-1} $\rightarrow$ \texttt{access-node} $\rightarrow$ \texttt{radius-splunk}
\end{center}

Within this path:

\begin{itemize}
    \item \texttt{client-1} acts as the wired supplicant;
    \item \texttt{access-node} acts as the Linux-based wired 802.1X authenticator using \texttt{hostapd};
    \item \texttt{radius-splunk} hosts FreeRADIUS as the authentication and policy service.
\end{itemize}

The incident was defined as a wired 802.1X authentication failure caused by invalid supplicant credentials. This was not an accidental outage but a controlled failure injection introduced for the purpose of validating the lab's ability to:

\begin{itemize}
    \item detect a failed NAC event;
    \item observe the failed state across the supplicant, authenticator, and RADIUS layers;
    \item document the event in an ITSM workflow;
    \item restore service using a known-good configuration;
    \item validate successful recovery through both host-side evidence and packet analysis.
\end{itemize}

The scenario therefore serves as a practical proof that the lab can do more than demonstrate component reachability. It can also support a realistic access-control failure narrative from symptom to closure.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 7.2: FAILURE INJECTION METHOD
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection documents the deliberate credential change used to
% produce the failed authentication state and explains why that method
% provides a controlled and reproducible endpoint-side failure.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{7.2 Failure Injection Method}
\label{sec:failure-injection-method}

The failure was introduced by deliberately replacing the correct wired supplicant password in the \texttt{client-1} \texttt{wpa\_supplicant} configuration with an incorrect value. This method was chosen because it is deterministic, easy to revert, and directly relevant to the type of endpoint-side credential error that could plausibly occur in an enterprise environment.

This approach has several advantages in a proof-of-concept lab:

\begin{itemize}
    \item it guarantees a failure condition at the authentication layer rather than at unrelated network layers;
    \item it preserves the normal routing and firewall design, isolating the incident to the NAC control path;
    \item it makes the remediation equally deterministic by restoring the known-good client configuration;
    \item it allows clean collection of failure and recovery evidence without introducing ambiguity about the cause of the event.
\end{itemize}

The lab therefore treats the credential modification as a formal failure-injection technique rather than as an incidental misconfiguration. This is important because it makes the incident reproducible and allows the later automation and packet-analysis workflows to operate against a known and explainable failure mode.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 7.3: AUTHORITATIVE INCIDENT ARTIFACTS
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection records the authoritative incident-evidence inventory
% and the parser summary used to synthesize the failure and recovery
% artifacts across the supplicant, authenticator, and RADIUS layers.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{7.3 Authoritative Incident Artifacts}
\label{sec:authoritative-incident-artifacts}

The incident evidence set is maintained on \texttt{radius-splunk} under the local incident-evidence directory. The final directory inventory at the time of documentation is reproduced below.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% NOTE: This directory listing is included because it establishes the retained
% incident artifact set and the specific files that support the failure,
% remediation, and parser-based summary described in this section.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\begin{verbatim}
radius-splunk: ls -lh ~/incident-evidence
total 92K
-rw-rw-r-- 1 nac-lab nac-lab  32K Mar 23 04:02 freeradius_failed_auth_incident.txt
-rw-rw-r-- 1 nac-lab nac-lab  11K Mar 23 04:01 hostapd_failed_auth_incident.txt
-rw-rw-r-- 1 nac-lab nac-lab  19K Mar 23 04:01 hostapd_success_retest_v2.txt
-rw-r--r-- 1 root    root    3.5K Mar 24 09:52 nac_incident_parser.py
-rw-rw-r-- 1 nac-lab nac-lab  535 Mar 23 04:28 nac_incident_parser_success.txt
-rw-rw-r-- 1 nac-lab nac-lab 7.3K Mar 23 03:59 wpa_supplicant_failed_auth_incident.txt
-rw-rw-r-- 1 nac-lab nac-lab 8.4K Mar 23 03:59 wpa_supplicant_success_retest_v2.txt
\end{verbatim}

These files are sufficient to support the full narrative of failure and recovery across the endpoint, authenticator, and RADIUS layers. They also form the input set for the Python incident parser.

At the time of final documentation, the parser remained functional and produced the following authoritative summary:

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% NOTE: This parser output is included because it provides a concise cross-
% artifact summary of the incident evidence and captures the final
% operational conclusion produced by the Python validation tool.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\begin{verbatim}
radius-splunk: python3 ~/incident-evidence/nac_incident_parser.py
=== NAC Incident Evidence Summary ===

[1] Failed authentication simulation
- Client remained unauthorized: YES
- Client showed successful connection during failed test: NO
- hostapd saw authentication attempt: YES
- FreeRADIUS recorded failed auth / reject path: YES

[2] Remediation / successful re-test
- Client reached CTRL-EVENT-CONNECTED: YES
- Client reached Authorized state: YES
- hostapd showed successful authorization indicators: YES

[3] Incident conclusion
- SIMULATED NAC INCIDENT SUCCESSFULLY REPRODUCED AND REMEDIATED
\end{verbatim}

This parser output is significant because it provides a deterministic, cross-file synthesis of the incident evidence. It does not replace the underlying artifacts, but it does provide a concise operational summary that agrees with the raw evidence reproduced below.

The full parser source is maintained separately as a platform artifact in Appendix A (see Section~\ref{sec:appendix-a-python-nac-incident-parser}).

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 7.4: FAILED AUTHENTICATION EVIDENCE
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection establishes the failed state across the three control
% points in the authentication path so that the incident is supported by
% independent endpoint, authenticator, and RADIUS evidence.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{7.4 Failed Authentication Evidence}
\label{sec:failed-authentication-evidence}

The failed state is established independently at all three control points: the supplicant, the authenticator, and the RADIUS service.

\subsubsection{7.4.1 Supplicant-Side Failed State}
\label{sec:supplicant-side-failed-state}

On the endpoint side, the failed-authentication capture shows that the supplicant remained unauthorized and never transitioned into a connected or authorized state.

\begin{verbatim}
client-side failed indicators
EAPOL: Supplicant port status: Unauthorized
EAPOL: Supplicant port status: Unauthorized
\end{verbatim}

This is the clearest endpoint-side symptom of the failure. There is no successful connection indicator in the failed-authentication evidence set.

\subsubsection{7.4.2 Authenticator-Side Failed State}
\label{sec:authenticator-side-failed-state}

On the authenticator side, \texttt{hostapd} recorded repeated attempts to send the authentication transaction toward the RADIUS server, confirming that the endpoint was actively attempting authentication and that the authenticator was processing the request path.

\begin{verbatim}
hostapd failed-auth indicators
Mar 22 22:32:33 access-node hostapd[2052]: ens38: RADIUS Sending RADIUS message to 
        authentication server
Mar 22 22:32:33 access-node hostapd[2052]: ens38: RADIUS Sending RADIUS message to 
        authentication server
Mar 22 22:32:33 access-node hostapd[2052]: ens38: RADIUS Sending RADIUS message to 
        authentication server
Mar 22 22:32:33 access-node hostapd[2052]: ens38: RADIUS Sending RADIUS message to 
        authentication server
\end{verbatim}

This is operationally important because it shows that the failure was not caused by a silent client or by an absence of authenticator activity. The authenticator was actively participating in the 802.1X control path and attempting to obtain a RADIUS decision.

\subsubsection{7.4.3 RADIUS-Side Failed State}
\label{sec:radius-side-failed-state}

The strongest proof of the failure appears in the FreeRADIUS evidence. The failed-authentication excerpt shows an EAP failure, rejection of the authentication attempt, and the use of the reject post-auth path.

\begin{verbatim}
FreeRADIUS failed-auth indicators
(1) eap: Sending EAP Failure (code 4) ID 26 length 4
(1)     [eap] = reject
(1)   } # authenticate = reject
(1) Failed to authenticate the user
(1) Using Post-Auth-Type Reject
(1)   Post-Auth-Type REJECT {
(1) attr_filter.access_reject: EXPAND %{User-Name}
(1) attr_filter.access_reject:    --> nacuser
(1) attr_filter.access_reject: Matched entry DEFAULT at line 11
(1)     [attr_filter.access_reject] = updated
(1)   } # Post-Auth-Type REJECT = updated
\end{verbatim}

This confirms that the failed state is not merely an endpoint perception. The policy authority itself rejected the authentication request and generated the expected EAP failure path.

Taken together, the endpoint, authenticator, and RADIUS evidence establish the failed state conclusively.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 7.5: INVESTIGATION AND TRIAGE
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection summarizes the investigation logic used to distinguish
% among endpoint, authenticator, and RADIUS failure domains and records
% the evidence-based triage conclusions reached during the incident.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{7.5 Investigation and Triage}
\label{sec:investigation-and-triage}

The investigation phase in this incident was intentionally simple but operationally credible. The goal was to distinguish among the most likely causes of a failed wired 802.1X event:

\begin{itemize}
    \item an endpoint-side credential or supplicant configuration error;
    \item an authenticator-side service or forwarding problem;
    \item a RADIUS-side policy or service failure.
\end{itemize}

The incident evidence and the surrounding validation steps supported the following triage conclusions:

\begin{itemize}
    \item the endpoint remained unauthorized, so the event was not a mere transient or cosmetic log anomaly;
    \item \texttt{hostapd} was actively sending RADIUS requests, which meant the authenticator path was engaged;
    \item FreeRADIUS was running and able to produce a deterministic reject path, which meant the backend service was operational;
    \item the known-good user credentials still produced Access-Accept under \texttt{radtest}, isolating the problem to the client-side credential state rather than to the FreeRADIUS policy model.
\end{itemize}

This investigation logic is reflected directly in the GLPI incident record, which documents verification of FreeRADIUS health, confirmation that the known-good credentials still produced Access-Accept, and revalidation of the client \texttt{wpa\_supplicant} configuration.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 7.6: REMEDIATION ACTIONS
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection documents the targeted remediation applied to restore
% the known-good supplicant credential state on client-1 and re-run the
% authentication path without broader architectural changes.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{7.6 Remediation Actions}
\label{sec:remediation-actions}

The remediation was deliberately minimal and targeted. Rather than changing the architecture, altering the firewall rules, or modifying RADIUS policy, the known-good \texttt{wpa\_supplicant} credential state on \texttt{client-1} was restored and the wired 802.1X authentication test was executed again under controlled conditions.

This remediation method is significant because it demonstrates a proper engineering response to a localized failure:

\begin{itemize}
    \item identify the probable failing control point;
    \item restore known-good state at that control point;
    \item re-test the full path rather than assuming success;
    \item validate the result across multiple evidence sources.
\end{itemize}

No broad changes to routing, pfSense policy, or FreeRADIUS user logic were required to restore service. That outcome is itself meaningful because it shows the failure was introduced and resolved at the endpoint credential layer rather than by systemic platform instability.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 7.7: SUCCESSFUL RE-TEST EVIDENCE
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection documents the successful recovery validation and shows
% that the remediated endpoint returned to connected and authorized
% state across the same control points used to establish the failure.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{7.7 Successful Re-Test Evidence}
\label{sec:successful-re-test-evidence}

The remediation was validated through a clean success re-test. As with the failed state, the successful state is visible at all three control points.

\subsubsection{7.7.1 Supplicant-Side Recovery Evidence}
\label{sec:supplicant-side-recovery-evidence}

The successful re-test capture shows the expected transition from initial unauthorized state to connected and authorized state, followed by a later return to unauthorized state when the controlled test process ended.

\begin{verbatim}
client-side success indicators
EAPOL: Supplicant port status: Unauthorized
ens33: CTRL-EVENT-CONNECTED - Connection to <REDACTED> completed [id=0 id_str=]
EAPOL: Supplicant port status: Authorized
EAPOL: Supplicant port status: Unauthorized
\end{verbatim}

The final \texttt{Unauthorized} line is not interpreted as a failed remediation. It is consistent with termination of the controlled test process after the successful exchange had already been completed. The decisive indicators are \texttt{CTRL-EVENT-CONNECTED} and \texttt{Authorized}.

\subsubsection{7.7.2 Authenticator-Side Recovery Evidence}
\label{sec:authenticator-side-recovery-evidence}

The successful re-test on the authenticator shows the expected sequence of RADIUS interaction, Access-Accept handling, EAP Success decapsulation, station connection, and port authorization.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% NOTE: This hostapd excerpt is included because it preserves the full
% authenticator-side success sequence from upstream RADIUS handling
% through EAP Success, station connection, and controlled-port
% authorization.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\begin{verbatim}
hostapd success indicators
Mar 23 02:49:00 access-node hostapd[2052]: Encapsulating EAP message into a RADIUS 
        packet
Mar 23 02:49:00 access-node hostapd[2052]: Copied RADIUS State Attribute
Mar 23 02:49:00 access-node hostapd[2052]: ens38: RADIUS Sending RADIUS message to 
        authentication server
Mar 23 02:49:00 access-node hostapd[2052]: RADIUS message: code=1 (Access-Request) 
        identifier=4 length=193
Mar 23 02:49:00 access-node hostapd[2052]: ens38: STA <REDACTED> IEEE 802.1X: 
        unauthorizing port
Mar 23 02:49:00 access-node hostapd[2052]: ens38: RADIUS Next RADIUS client retransmit in 
        3 seconds
Mar 23 02:49:00 access-node hostapd[2052]: ens38: RADIUS Received 53 bytes from RADIUS 
        server
Mar 23 02:49:00 access-node hostapd[2052]: ens38: RADIUS Received RADIUS message
Mar 23 02:49:00 access-node hostapd[2052]: RADIUS message: code=2 (Access-Accept) 
        identifier=4 length=53
Mar 23 02:49:00 access-node hostapd[2052]: ens38: STA <REDACTED> RADIUS: Received 
        RADIUS packet matched with a pending request, round trip time 0.00 sec
Mar 23 02:49:00 access-node hostapd[2052]: RADIUS packet matching with station 
        <REDACTED>
Mar 23 02:49:00 access-node hostapd[2052]: ens38: STA <REDACTED> IEEE 802.1X: 
        decapsulated EAP packet (code=3 id=255 len=4) from RADIUS server: EAP Success
Mar 23 02:49:00 access-node hostapd[2052]: ens38: CTRL-EVENT-EAP-SUCCESS2 <REDACTED>
Mar 23 02:49:00 access-node hostapd[2052]: ens38: AP-STA-CONNECTED <REDACTED>
Mar 23 02:49:00 access-node hostapd[2052]: ens38: STA <REDACTED> IEEE 802.1X: 
        authorizing port
Mar 23 02:49:00 access-node hostapd[2052]: ens38: STA <REDACTED> RADIUS: starting 
        accounting session 2268479955CB85A0
\end{verbatim}

This is the strongest authenticator-side proof of successful remediation. The sequence explicitly shows:
- RADIUS request handling,
- Access-Accept reception,
- EAP Success,
- station connection,
- and port authorization.

\subsubsection{7.7.3 RADIUS-Side Recovery Interpretation}
\label{sec:radius-side-recovery-interpretation}

A dedicated successful FreeRADIUS transaction excerpt was not preserved in the same compact text form as the failed-authentication reject path. However, this does not weaken the conclusion because the success state is already corroborated by:

\begin{itemize}
    \item the endpoint-side connected and authorized state;
    \item the authenticator-side Access-Accept and EAP Success handling;
    \item the parser summary, which concluded that the incident was successfully reproduced and remediated.
\end{itemize}

In this case, the successful RADIUS outcome is most directly evidenced through the authenticator's decoding of the received RADIUS Accept and EAP Success path.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 7.8: GLPI INCIDENT RECORD
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection records the formal GLPI incident entry associated with
% the simulated authentication failure and ties the technical evidence
% to an operational ticketing workflow.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{7.8 GLPI Incident Record}
\label{sec:glpi-incident-record}

The incident was not left as a purely technical artifact. It was also recorded in GLPI as an actual operational record associated with the lab asset model. This is important because it demonstrates that the environment integrates ITSM process with technical evidence rather than treating ticketing as an unrelated side service.

At the time of documentation, the incident ticket title and status were as follows:

\begin{verbatim}
Ticket title:
Wired 802.1X authentication failure and remediation for client-1

Ticket status:
Solved
\end{verbatim}

The full incident text recorded in GLPI is reproduced below.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% NOTE: This GLPI incident text is reproduced because it captures the
% formal operational record of the simulated event, including observed
% behavior, investigation steps, remediation actions, and recovery
% validation.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\begin{verbatim}
Incident summary:
client-1 failed wired 802.1X authentication during NAC validation after the supplicant 
        password was intentionally changed to an incorrect value to simulate a credential
        issue.

Observed behavior:
- client-1 remained unauthorized during the failed test
- FreeRADIUS showed a failed authentication / reject path
- hostapd on access-node recorded the 802.1X authentication attempt

Investigation:
- Verified FreeRADIUS service health on radius-splunk
- Confirmed the known-good credentials still produced Access-Accept using radtest
- Revalidated the client wpa_supplicant configuration

Remediation:
- Restored the known-good wpa_supplicant credentials on client-1
- Re-ran the wired 802.1X authentication test

Recovery validation:
- client-1 showed CTRL-EVENT-CONNECTED
- client-1 supplicant port reached Authorized state
- access-node hostapd logs showed AP-STA-CONNECTED and port authorization

Impact:
This simulated a realistic enterprise NAC incident involving endpoint credential failure 
        and demonstrated successful troubleshooting and service restoration.
\end{verbatim}

For associate GLPI GUI screenshots, see Section~\ref{sec:glpi-final-configuration}.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 7.9: INCIDENT CONCLUSION
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection closes the incident narrative by summarizing the full
% failure-and-recovery lifecycle and stating its significance for the
% lab as an operationally credible NAC validation environment.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{7.9 Incident Conclusion}
\label{sec:incident-conclusion}

The incident scenario demonstrates that the lab can support a complete wired NAC failure-and-recovery lifecycle:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item a controlled authentication failure was introduced at the endpoint credential layer;
    \item the endpoint remained unauthorized;
    \item the authenticator processed and forwarded the failed authentication path;
    \item FreeRADIUS generated the expected reject and EAP failure path;
    \item the issue was investigated without unnecessary architectural changes;
    \item the known-good client configuration was restored;
    \item a clean re-test demonstrated connected and authorized state;
    \item the event was recorded and closed in GLPI.
\end{enumerate}

This is one of the most important outcomes in the document because it transforms the lab from a static collection of services into an operational environment that can support:

\begin{itemize}
    \item failure simulation;
    \item layered evidence collection;
    \item repeatable remediation;
    \item cross-domain validation using routing, logging, ITSM, automation, and packet capture.
\end{itemize}

The later automation and packet-capture sections build directly on this incident narrative. The Python parser summarizes the same evidence set reproduced here, and the Wireshark screenshots provide protocol-level confirmation of both the failed and successful authentication paths.

% =====================================================================
% SECTION 8: AUTOMATION WORKFLOWS
% =====================================================================
% This section documents the automation layer implemented around the NAC
% lab. The goal is not to present automation as an isolated scripting
% exercise, but as an operational control capability supporting service
% validation, optional remediation, and evidence collection.
% =====================================================================

\section{8. Automation Workflows}
\label{sec:automation-workflows}

The lab includes a lightweight but operationally meaningful automation layer designed to support repeatable validation of the wired 802.1X control path and the associated authentication services. Rather than attempting to implement a full orchestration or SOAR platform, the automation focuses on three practical goals:

\begin{itemize}
    \item validate the state of the authenticator and RADIUS services from a central node;
    \item optionally remediate service availability by restarting critical daemons;
    \item preserve local evidence artifacts for later review and appendix inclusion.
\end{itemize}

This design is best characterized as validation-first with optional service remediation. That phrasing is deliberate. The automation is primarily an evidence and control workflow, not a blind auto-restart mechanism.

The automation controller for this lab is \texttt{radius-splunk}. From that node, Ansible is used to validate the Linux-based wired 802.1X authenticator on \texttt{access-node} and the FreeRADIUS service on \texttt{radius-splunk} itself. A separate Python parser is then used to summarize the incident evidence set collected during the failure-and-remediation scenario described in Section~\ref{sec:incident-scenario-wired-8021x-authentication-failure-and-remediation}.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 8.1: AUTOMATION ASSET INVENTORY

% retained artifact set, and the authoritative playbook and parser files
% used by the validation and incident-summary workflows.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 8.2: PLAYBOOK DESIGN AND REVISION RATIONALE
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection describes the final Ansible playbook design and the
% corrections that established the revised workflow as the authoritative
% implementation for validation, remediation, and artifact handling.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{8.2 Playbook Design and Revision Rationale}
\label{sec:playbook-design-and-revision-rationale}

The final Ansible workflow uses a single playbook, \texttt{nac\_validation.yml}, which was intentionally kept compact and understandable. Its functional responsibilities are:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item collect a basic hostname from each target;
    \item validate the \texttt{hostapd-wired.service} state on \texttt{access-node};
    \item validate the \texttt{freeradius.service} state on \texttt{radius-splunk};
    \item optionally restart one or both services when \texttt{remediate=true} is provided;
    \item re-check service state after optional remediation;
    \item collect recent journal excerpts and recent FreeRADIUS application log output;
    \item write all collected outputs into a local artifact directory on the control node.
\end{enumerate}

The playbook underwent a meaningful correction during final validation and the corrected version is treated as the authoritative implementation for this document.

Specifically, the final playbook:

\begin{itemize}
    \item sets \texttt{become: false} at the play scope;
    \item applies \texttt{become: true} only to tasks that require elevated privileges;
    \item uses a fixed \texttt{artifact\_dir} variable (\texttt{/home/nac-lab/ansible-nac/artifacts});
    \item explicitly sets \texttt{become: false} on delegated localhost copy tasks.
\end{itemize}

This revision is not a cosmetic change. It materially improves operational correctness by preventing two undesirable behaviors:

\begin{itemize}
    \item unnecessary sudo prompts for harmless non-privileged tasks;
    \item accidental dependence on the invoking user's \texttt{HOME} when writing delegated localhost artifacts.
\end{itemize}

In practical terms, the revised playbook makes execution deterministic and avoids the SSH and artifact-path ambiguity that can occur when operators attempt to run Ansible itself under \texttt{sudo}. The final document therefore treats the revised playbook as the production version of the automation workflow.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 8.3: VALIDATION-ONLY EXECUTION
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection documents the default validation-only playbook run and
% shows how the workflow checks service state and writes artifacts
% without attempting service restarts.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{8.3 Validation-Only Execution}
\label{sec:validation-only-execution}

The first execution mode validates service state without attempting remediation. This is the default behavior and is the recommended mode for routine health checks.

The command used was:

\begin{verbatim}
cd ~/ansible-nac
ansible-playbook -i inventory/hosts.ini playbooks/nac_validation.yml -K
\end{verbatim}

The authoritative execution output is reproduced below.

This run demonstrates the intended baseline behavior:

\begin{itemize}
    \item both managed nodes are reachable;
    \item the service validation tasks complete successfully;
    \item the remediation tasks are correctly skipped when not requested;
    \item evidence files are still written to the local artifact directory.
\end{itemize}

This is the preferred operational mode for routine health checks because it confirms service state and preserves evidence without making any changes to the environment.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 8.4: REMEDIATION-ENABLED EXECUTION
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection documents the remediation-enabled playbook run and
% shows the same validation workflow operating with controlled service
% restart actions on the managed nodes.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{8.4 Remediation-Enabled Execution}
\label{sec:remediation-enabled-execution}

The second execution mode enables controlled service remediation by setting \texttt{remediate=true}. In this mode, the playbook still performs the same validation and evidence collection steps, but it additionally restarts the managed services.

The command used was:

\begin{verbatim}
cd ~/ansible-nac
ansible-playbook -i inventory/hosts.ini playbooks/nac_validation.yml -K -e remediate=true
\end{verbatim}

The authoritative execution output is reproduced below.

This run confirms that the automation can perform controlled remediation without abandoning its evidence-preservation role. The restart tasks executed successfully on both nodes:

\begin{itemize}

    \item \texttt{freeradius.service} on \texttt{radius-splunk}.
\end{itemize}

The post-check tasks then completed successfully, which is essential. In a production-style workflow, the restart itself is not the success condition; the success condition is a successful post-remediation validation of the service state.

Tue Mar 24 11:34:18 2026 : Info: Loaded virtual server inner-tunnel
Tue Mar 24 11:34:18 2026 : Info: Loaded virtual server default
Tue Mar 24 11:34:18 2026 : Info: Ready to process requests
Tue Mar 24 11:57:46 2026 : Info: Signalled to terminate

===== /home/nac-lab/ansible-nac/artifacts/radius-splunk_freeradius_restart_result.txt =====
remediation not requested

===== /home/nac-lab/ansible-nac/artifacts/radius-splunk_freeradius_status_after.txt =====
- freeradius.service - FreeRADIUS multi-protocol policy server
     Loaded: loaded (/usr/lib/systemd/system/freeradius.service; enabled; preset: enabled)
     Active: active (running) since Tue 2026-03-24 12:23:12 UTC; 20min ago
       Docs: man:radiusd(8)
             man:radiusd.conf(5)
             http://wiki.freeradius.org/
             http://networkradius.com/doc/

===== /home/nac-lab/ansible-nac/artifacts/radius-splunk_freeradius_status_before.txt =====
- freeradius.service - FreeRADIUS multi-protocol policy server
     Loaded: loaded (/usr/lib/systemd/system/freeradius.service; enabled; preset: enabled)
     Active: active (running) since Tue 2026-03-24 12:23:12 UTC; 20min ago
       Docs: man:radiusd(8)
             man:radiusd.conf(5)
             http://wiki.freeradius.org/
             http://networkradius.com/doc/

===== /home/nac-lab/ansible-nac/artifacts/radius-splunk_freeradius_status.txt =====
- freeradius.service - FreeRADIUS multi-protocol policy server
     Loaded: loaded (/usr/lib/systemd/system/freeradius.service; enabled; preset: enabled)
     Active: active (running) since Mon 2026-03-23 18:52:42 UTC; 37min ago
       Docs: man:radiusd(8)
             man:radiusd.conf(5)
             http://wiki.freeradius.org/
             http://networkradius.com/doc/
\end{verbatim}

Several observations are worth recording:

\begin{itemize}
    \item the artifact files are human-readable and immediately useful without additional tooling;
    \item the \texttt{hostapd} journal excerpt clearly captures a service deinitialization path consistent with a controlled restart;
    \item the FreeRADIUS journal excerpt captures a stop/start lifecycle and successful service initialization;
    \item the service status files provide simple before/after validation anchors;
    \item the legacy \texttt{*\_status.txt} files remain present as earlier-generation artifacts, while the newer \texttt{*\_status\_before.txt} and \texttt{*\_status\_after.txt} files reflect the final workflow.
\end{itemize}

One nuance should be noted: the restart result files currently contain \texttt{remediation not requested}. This reflects the playbook's current use of \texttt{stdout} capture for the restart tasks. Because \texttt{systemctl restart} does not normally emit meaningful stdout, the fallback string remains in place even during a successful remediation-enabled run. This does \emph{not} invalidate the workflow, because the authoritative proof of remediation is instead found in:

\begin{itemize}
    \item the Ansible console output showing the restart tasks as \texttt{changed};
    \item the post-check service status files;
    \item the journal excerpts showing stop/start lifecycle behavior.
\end{itemize}

This limitation is acceptable in the current lab and will be addressed explicitly later in the limitations section.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 8.6: PYTHON INCIDENT PARSER ROLE
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection documents the role of the Python incident parser as a
% narrow evidence-summary tool that complements the Ansible workflow by
% synthesizing the preserved incident artifacts.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{8.6 Python Incident Parser Role}
\label{sec:python-incident-parser-role}

The Ansible playbook is not the only automation artifact in the environment. A separate Python utility, \texttt{nac\_incident\_parser.py}, is used to summarize the incident evidence files collected during the controlled failure-and-remediation scenario.

At the time of final documentation, the parser executed successfully and produced the following authoritative output:

\begin{verbatim}
radius-splunk: python3 ~/incident-evidence/nac_incident_parser.py
=== NAC Incident Evidence Summary ===

[1] Failed authentication simulation
- Client remained unauthorized: YES
- Client showed successful connection during failed test: NO
- hostapd saw authentication attempt: YES
- FreeRADIUS recorded failed auth / reject path: YES

[2] Remediation / successful re-test
- Client reached CTRL-EVENT-CONNECTED: YES
- Client reached Authorized state: YES
- hostapd showed successful authorization indicators: YES

[3] Incident conclusion
- SIMULATED NAC INCIDENT SUCCESSFULLY REPRODUCED AND REMEDIATED
\end{verbatim}

This parser is intentionally narrow in scope. It is not a general log analytics engine and it does not replace direct review of raw evidence. Instead, it provides a lightweight operational summary that is useful for:

\begin{itemize}
    \item quickly confirming that the expected failure and recovery markers are present;
    \item reducing manual re-check time during repeated test cycles;
    \item supporting a validation narrative that remains grounded in raw evidence files.
\end{itemize}

The parser therefore complements the Ansible workflow rather than competing with it:

\begin{itemize}
    \item Ansible validates service state and captures artifacts;
    \item the Python parser summarizes the incident evidence set;
    \item both workflows remain transparent and auditable because their underlying inputs are preserved.
\end{itemize}

The full parser source is reproduced in Appendix A (see Section~\ref{sec:appendix-a-python-nac-incident-parser}).

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 8.7: OPERATIONAL ASSESSMENT
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection evaluates the automation layer as an operational
% control pattern and explains how the final workflow supports repeatable
% validation, bounded remediation, and retained evidence collection.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{8.7 Operational Assessment}
\label{sec:operational-assessment}

From an operational perspective, the automation layer succeeds because it does not overreach. It implements a realistic control pattern for a lab of this size:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item validate the relevant services;
    \item optionally restart them when instructed;
    \item immediately re-check state;
    \item persist artifacts locally;
    \item correlate with separate incident evidence.
\end{enumerate}

During final validation:

\begin{itemize}
    \item both playbook runs completed successfully without manual intervention during execution;
    \item SSH connectivity from \texttt{radius-splunk} to \texttt{access-node} remained stable during successful runs;
    \item both the validation-only and remediation-enabled workflows completed without unreachable or failed hosts.
\end{itemize}

This is intended to demonstrate:

\begin{itemize}
    \item operational thinking rather than one-time manual testing;
    \item repeatability across service validation cycles;
    \item controlled remediation rather than purely observational monitoring;
    \item artifact preservation suitable for documentation and audit-style review.
\end{itemize}

At the same time, the automation remains intentionally modest. The purpose of the automation is to demonstrate sound operational design in a constrained environment, not to claim parity with enterprise orchestration platforms.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 8.8: SECTION CONCLUSION
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection closes the automation section by summarizing the role
% of the playbook and parser in strengthening the lab as a repeatable
% and operationally credible NAC validation environment.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{8.8 Section Conclusion}
\label{sec:section-8-conclusion}

The automation layer improves the robustness of the lab as an enterprise-aligned production artifact.

Without automation, the lab would still demonstrate a valid wired 802.1X design, but it would remain largely manual. With the addition of Ansible-based validation and optional remediation, plus the Python evidence parser, the lab demonstrates a more complete operational model:

\begin{itemize}
    \item services can be checked from a central node;
    \item remediation can be invoked deliberately rather than manually per host;
    \item artifacts are preserved locally for review;
    \item incident evidence can be summarized in a repeatable way;
    \item the automation remains transparent, bounded, and easy to audit.
\end{itemize}

This is the correct level of automation for the environment. It supports the lab's central goals without obscuring the underlying technical controls or replacing direct evidence review.

% =====================================================================
% SECTION 9: OBSERVABILITY AND LOGGING PIPELINE
% =====================================================================
% This section documents the local log sources, monitored-input
% configuration, service-specific evidence paths, and the role of
% curated incident capture files in the final lab.
% =====================================================================

\section{9. Observability and Logging Pipeline}
\label{sec:observability-and-logging-pipeline}

The lab includes a deliberately structured observability layer intended to support both steady-state validation and incident-focused troubleshooting. This layer is built from a combination of:

\begin{itemize}
    \item continuously generated local service and system logs;
    \item service-oriented journald output for the Linux-based authenticator;
    \item Splunk Universal Forwarder monitored-input configuration;
    \item manually curated incident evidence files generated during controlled failure and recovery testing.
\end{itemize}

The objective is not to claim a full centralized SIEM deployment, but rather to demonstrate that the environment has a coherent, technically meaningful evidence pipeline. In this lab, observability is treated as an engineering function in its own right: the system produces logs, those logs are mapped to identifiable sources, relevant paths are monitored, and incident-specific captures are preserved separately when the continuously generated logs alone are insufficient for a clean narrative.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 9.1: OBSERVABILITY SCOPE AND POSITIONING
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection defines the scope of the observability layer and
% positions the Splunk component as a local monitored-input deployment
% rather than a full centralized SIEM architecture.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{9.1 Observability Scope and Positioning}
\label{sec:observability-scope-and-positioning}

The Splunk component in this lab is deliberately scoped as a local monitored-input configuration without demonstrated remote indexer or search-head integration. That scope is both accurate and sufficient for the environment that was built.

This section therefore demonstrates the following, and only the following:

\begin{itemize}
    \item identification of the relevant local log sources;
    \item configuration of Splunk Universal Forwarder to monitor those sources;
    \item preservation of service and incident evidence in local files;
    \item a logging model that supports the final-state validation and incident sections of this document.
\end{itemize}

It does \emph{not} demonstrate:

\begin{itemize}
    \item centralized Splunk search or analytics;
    \item a remote indexer/search-head topology;
    \item dashboards, alerts, or correlation searches;
    \item enterprise retention, forwarding, or multi-tier ingestion architecture.
\end{itemize}

This scope boundary matters because it allows the document to make a strong but accurate claim: the lab is instrumented and observable, even though it is not a full SIEM deployment.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 9.2: SPLUNK UNIVERSAL FORWARDER MONITORED INPUTS
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection documents the active Splunk Universal Forwarder state,
% the local monitored-input configuration, and the specific log sources
% selected for continuous monitoring on radius-splunk.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{9.2 Splunk Universal Forwarder Monitored Inputs}
\label{sec:splunk-universal-forwarder-monitored-inputs}

The Splunk Universal Forwarder on \texttt{radius-splunk} is active in the final state:

\begin{verbatim}
radius-splunk: /opt/splunkforwarder/bin/splunk status
Warning: Attempting to revert the SPLUNK_HOME ownership
Warning: Executing "chown -R root /opt/splunkforwarder"
splunkd is running (PID: 1489)
splunk helpers are running (PIDs: 1490)
\end{verbatim}

The monitored-input configuration is maintained in a dedicated local app:

\begin{verbatim}
radius-splunk: /opt/splunkforwarder/etc/apps/lab_nac_inputs/local/inputs.conf
[monitor:///var/log/syslog]
disabled = false
index = main
sourcetype = syslog

[monitor:///var/log/auth.log]
disabled = false
index = main
sourcetype = linux_secure

[monitor:///var/log/freeradius/radius.log]
disabled = false
index = main
sourcetype = freeradius
\end{verbatim}

The app layout itself is minimal and clean:

\begin{verbatim}
radius-splunk: find /opt/splunkforwarder/etc/apps/lab_nac_inputs -maxdepth 3 -type f | sort
/opt/splunkforwarder/etc/apps/lab_nac_inputs/local/inputs.conf
\end{verbatim}

This is the correct level of Splunk configuration for the lab. The forwarder monitors three sources that are directly relevant to the final environment:

\begin{itemize}
    \item \texttt{/var/log/syslog} for general system and service lifecycle events;
    \item \texttt{/var/log/auth.log} for administrative access and privilege activity;
    \item \texttt{/var/log/freeradius/radius.log} for FreeRADIUS application-layer events.
\end{itemize}

Together, these monitored inputs provide coverage over:
- host operating-system state,
- administrative activity,
- and the primary authentication service itself.

The full UF input configuration is reproduced in Appendix A (see Section~\ref{sec:appendix-a-splunk-uf-inputs-conf}).

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

% This subsection documents the primary continuously generated log

% FreeRADIUS application logs contribute to the observability layer.

\subsection{9.3 Primary Continuous Log Sources on \texttt{radius-splunk}}
\label{sec:primary-continuous-log-sources-on-radius-splunk}

The most important continuously generated log sources in the environment are located on \texttt{radius-splunk}. These include system log data, authentication/admin activity, and FreeRADIUS application output.

Their current filesystem metadata is summarized below.

\begin{verbatim}
radius-splunk log files

auth.log: -rw-r----- 1 syslog adm 233K Mar 24 13:25 /var/log/auth.log
radius.log: -rw-r--r-- 1 syslog adm 12K Mar 24 13:14 /var/log/freeradius/radius.log
\end{verbatim}

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 9.4: HOSTAPD OBSERVABILITY ON ACCESS-NODE
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection documents the observability path for hostapd on
% access-node and explains that its authoritative continuous evidence is
% exposed through the systemd service state and journal rather than a
% dedicated application log file.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{9.4 \texttt{hostapd} Observability on \texttt{access-node}}
\label{sec:hostapd-observability-on-access-node}

Unlike FreeRADIUS, the Linux-based authenticator does not rely on a dedicated application log file under \texttt{/var/log}. Instead, its authoritative operational evidence is exposed through the managed systemd service and the journal.

The current service state is reproduced below.

\begin{verbatim}
access-node: systemctl status hostapd-wired.service --no-pager
Loaded: loaded (/etc/systemd/system/hostapd-wired.service; enabled; preset: enabled)
Active: active (running) since Tue 2026-03-24 04:20:41 UTC
Main PID: 975 (hostapd)

Recent lines:
Using interface ens38 with hwaddr <REDACTED> and ssid ""
ens38: RADIUS Authentication server 10.0.30.10:1812
RADIUS local address: 10.0.23.1:45833
ens38: interface state UNINITIALIZED->ENABLED
ens38: AP-ENABLED
ens38: Setup of interface done.
\end{verbatim}

The underlying unit file is located at:

\begin{verbatim}
access-node: systemctl show -p FragmentPath hostapd-wired.service
FragmentPath=/etc/systemd/system/hostapd-wired.service
\end{verbatim}

A representative journal excerpt is reproduced below.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% NOTE: This journal excerpt is included because it preserves recent
% hostapd lifecycle and startup events from the authoritative continuous
% log source used by the authenticator in this environment.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\begin{verbatim}

        /etc/hostapd/lab/hostapd-wired.conf

        (0 bits)

        channel/rate support not supported.
