Mar 24 04:20:41 access-node hostapd[975]: ens38: AP-ENABLED
Mar 24 04:20:41 access-node hostapd[975]: ens38: Setup of interface done.
Mar 24 04:20:41 access-node hostapd[975]: ctrl_iface not configured!
\end{verbatim}

No dedicated \texttt{hostapd} file was present under \texttt{/var/log} at the time of validation. This is an important architectural note because it clarifies that \texttt{journald} is the authoritative continuous log source for the Linux-based authenticator in this environment.

This distinction matters operationally:
- FreeRADIUS provides both service/journal context and a dedicated application log file;
- \texttt{hostapd} primarily provides service/journal context;
- both are still compatible with the incident evidence workflow because curated captures can be created from their runtime output when a more focused artifact is needed.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 9.5: CURATED INCIDENT EVIDENCE AS A COMPLEMENTARY LAYER
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection documents the curated incident-evidence directory and
% explains how manually captured debug artifacts complement the baseline
% continuous logging sources during controlled failure and recovery work.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{9.5 Curated Incident Evidence as a Complementary Layer}
\label{sec:curated-incident-evidence-as-a-complementary-layer}

The continuously generated logs described above are necessary, but they are not the only observability artifacts in the lab. The environment also includes a curated incident-evidence directory on \texttt{radius-splunk}:

\begin{verbatim}
radius-splunk: ls -lh ~/incident-evidence
total 92K
-rw-rw-r-- 1 nac-lab nac-lab  32K Mar 23 04:02 freeradius_failed_auth_incident.txt
-rw-rw-r-- 1 nac-lab nac-lab  11K Mar 23 04:01 hostapd_failed_auth_incident.txt
-rw-rw-r-- 1 nac-lab nac-lab  19K Mar 23 04:01 hostapd_success_retest_v2.txt
-rw-r--r-- 1 root    root    3.5K Mar 24 09:52 nac_incident_parser.py
-rw-rw-r-- 1 nac-lab nac-lab  535 Mar 23 04:28 nac_incident_parser_success.txt
-rw-rw-r-- 1 nac-lab nac-lab 7.3K Mar 23 03:59 wpa_supplicant_failed_auth_incident.txt
-rw-rw-r-- 1 nac-lab nac-lab 8.4K Mar 23 03:59 wpa_supplicant_success_retest_v2.txt
\end{verbatim}

The following files were explicitly confirmed as manually captured from debug or controlled test runs:

\begin{itemize}
    \item \texttt{wpa\_supplicant\_failed\_auth\_incident.txt};
    \item \texttt{wpa\_supplicant\_success\_retest\_v2.txt};
    \item \texttt{hostapd\_failed\_auth\_incident.txt};
    \item \texttt{hostapd\_success\_retest\_v2.txt};
    \item \texttt{freeradius\_failed\_auth\_incident.txt}.
\end{itemize}

This distinction should be made explicit:

\begin{itemize}
    \item the continuously generated system and service logs are the baseline observability layer;
    \item the incident-evidence files are curated captures created to preserve high-signal artifacts from controlled validation runs.
\end{itemize}

The incident evidence files therefore complement rather than replace the continuous logging sources. They are particularly useful when the goal is to preserve a deterministic failure or recovery narrative in a form that is easy to reference later in the document, the parser, and the appendix sections.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 9.6: OBSERVABILITY VALUE TO THE LAB
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection explains how the combined logging, journald, monitored-
% input, and curated-evidence model supports the validation, incident,
% automation, and packet-analysis sections that follow.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{9.6 Observability Value to the Lab}
\label{sec:observability-value-to-the-lab}

The observability design in this lab is modest in scale but strong in structure. It provides:

\begin{itemize}
    \item identifiable local log sources for system, administrative, and authentication behavior;
    \item a dedicated monitored-input configuration in Splunk Universal Forwarder;
    \item service-oriented journal visibility for the Linux authenticator;
    \item curated incident evidence files for deterministic reproduction and documentation.
\end{itemize}

This combination is what makes later sections of the document possible. The access-control validation section depends on service and routing evidence. The incident section depends on curated captures and service logs. The automation section depends on local evidence persistence. The packet-analysis section depends on protocol captures aligned with the same operational story.

In other words, the observability layer is not an afterthought. It is one of the elements that makes the lab function as a coherent engineering artifact rather than as a loose set of services.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 9.7: SECTION CONCLUSION
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection closes the observability section by summarizing the
% logging model as a well-scoped local evidence pipeline with clearly
% stated bounds on what the Splunk component does and does not represent.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{9.7 Section Conclusion}
\label{sec:section-9-conclusion}

The final lab demonstrates a well-scoped but credible logging and observability model.

It is accurate to say that the environment includes:

\begin{itemize}
    \item log source identification;
    \item log capture and monitored-input configuration;
    \item service-level journal visibility;
    \item evidence preservation for validation and incident analysis.
\end{itemize}

It would not be accurate to claim that the environment demonstrates centralized Splunk search or enterprise analytics. The document will therefore continue to describe the Splunk component precisely as a local UF-based monitored-input layer.

% =====================================================================
% SECTION 10: PACKET CAPTURE ANALYSIS (TCPDUMP + WIRESHARK)
% =====================================================================
% This section documents protocol-level validation of the simulated
% wired 802.1X incident and the successful remediation re-test.
% It correlates the client-facing EAPOL exchange with the upstream
% authenticator-to-RADIUS transaction so that the incident is validated
% not only through host logs and service state, but also through direct
% packet evidence at both the IEEE 802.1X and RADIUS layers.
% =====================================================================

\section{10. Packet Capture Analysis (tcpdump + Wireshark)}
\label{sec:packet-capture-analysis-tcpdump-and-wireshark}

Packet capture analysis was performed to validate the incident scenario at the protocol layer. Earlier sections established the operational facts of the incident through endpoint behavior, FreeRADIUS evidence, hostapd evidence, and GLPI ticketing. This section adds a stricter form of proof by showing that the failed and successful authentication paths are directly observable in packet capture.

This is important because it demonstrates that the incident was not inferred only from logs or service state. The captures show the actual IEEE~802.1X / EAPOL exchange on the client-facing segment and the corresponding RADIUS transaction on the upstream path. In effect, the packet evidence ties together the supplicant, the authenticator, and the AAA backend into one coherent protocol narrative.

The scope of the capture work was intentionally narrow. These were not broad traffic captures intended to inventory all host communications. They were short, purpose-built captures limited to the authentication window so that the relevant control-plane exchange would remain concise, readable, and suitable for publication.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 10.1: CAPTURE METHODOLOGY
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection defines where the packet captures were taken, which
% interfaces were used, and why the selected observation points are
% sufficient to correlate the client-facing IEEE 802.1X exchange with
% the upstream RADIUS transaction.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{10.1 Capture Methodology}
\label{sec:capture-methodology}

All packet captures for this section were taken on \texttt{access-node}. This was the appropriate capture point because \texttt{access-node} is the wired 802.1X authenticator and therefore sits at the boundary between the client-facing controlled port and the upstream routed path toward FreeRADIUS.

Two interfaces were used:

\begin{itemize}
    \item \texttt{ens38} --- wired 802.1X / EAPOL side toward \texttt{client-1}
    \item \texttt{ens39} --- routed upstream side toward \texttt{dist-router} and the RADIUS service path
\end{itemize}

This dual-observation approach makes it possible to correlate:
\begin{itemize}
    \item the client-facing authentication result seen as EAPOL / EAP messages, and
    \item the backend AAA decision seen as RADIUS request/challenge/accept/reject exchange.
\end{itemize}

That correlation is the central analytical value of the packet capture work in this lab.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 10.2: CAPTURE INVENTORY
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection establishes the authoritative pcap inventory used by
% the packet-analysis section and records the retained capture files
% and their sizes on access-node.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{10.2 Capture Inventory}
\label{sec:capture-inventory}

The authoritative packet capture inventory consists of the following four files:

\begin{itemize}
    \item \texttt{failed\_8021x\_ens38.pcap}
    \item \texttt{failed\_8021x\_ens39.pcap}
    \item \texttt{success\_8021x\_ens38.pcap}
    \item \texttt{success\_8021x\_ens39.pcap}
\end{itemize}

The pcap inventory on \texttt{access-node} was as follows:

\begin{verbatim}
access-node: ls -lh ~/pcaps
-rw-r--r-- 1 nac-lab nac-lab 385 Mar 23 20:23 failed_8021x_ens38.pcap
-rw-r--r-- 1 nac-lab nac-lab 738 Mar 23 20:23 failed_8021x_ens39.pcap
-rw-r--r-- 1 nac-lab nac-lab 385 Mar 23 20:29 success_8021x_ens38.pcap
-rw-r--r-- 1 nac-lab nac-lab 747 Mar 23 20:29 success_8021x_ens39.pcap
\end{verbatim}

The small file sizes are expected and desirable. They indicate that the captures were intentionally constrained to the short authentication test windows rather than left running as general packet traces.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 10.3: CAPTURE PROCEDURE
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection records the authoritative tcpdump capture procedure
% and the controlled manual supplicant invocation used to create the
% failed and successful authentication windows analyzed in this section.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{10.3 Capture Procedure}
\label{sec:capture-procedure}

The captures were obtained by starting two concurrent \texttt{tcpdump} processes on \texttt{access-node} and then forcing a controlled supplicant authentication attempt from \texttt{client-1}. The method used was as follows:

\begin{verbatim}
access-node:
sudo timeout 20s tcpdump -i ens38 -w /tmp/failed_8021x_ens38.pcap ether proto 0x888e &
sudo timeout 20s tcpdump -i ens39 -w /tmp/failed_8021x_ens39.pcap host 10.0.30.10 and 
        udp port 1812 &
sleep 2

client-1:
sudo systemctl stop wpa_supplicant
sudo timeout 15s wpa_supplicant -i ens33 -D wired -c 
        /etc/wpa_supplicant/lab/wired-8021x.conf -dd

access-node:
sudo timeout 20s tcpdump -i ens38 -w ~/pcaps/success_8021x_ens38.pcap ether proto 0x888e &
sudo timeout 20s tcpdump -i ens39 -w ~/pcaps/success_8021x_ens39.pcap host 10.0.30.10 and
        udp port 1812 &
sleep 2

client-1:
sudo systemctl stop wpa_supplicant
sudo timeout 15s wpa_supplicant -i ens33 -D wired -c 
        /etc/wpa_supplicant/lab/wired-8021x.conf -dd
\end{verbatim}

This method is appropriate for a controlled lab because it produces deterministic, low-noise captures focused only on the authentication event under investigation.

The authoritative capture commands and retained packet inventory will be reproduced in full in Appendix~A (see Section~\ref{sec:appendix-b-packet-capture-methodology-and-tcpdump-commands} and Section~\ref{sec:appendix-b-pcap-file-inventory}).

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 10.4: FAILED AUTHENTICATION - CLIENT-FACING EAPOL ANALYSIS
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection analyzes the failed client-facing packet capture on
% the controlled port and establishes that the endpoint participated in
% a complete IEEE 802.1X / EAP exchange that terminated explicitly with
% EAP Failure rather than link loss or absent negotiation.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{10.4 Failed Authentication: Client-Facing EAPOL Analysis}
\label{sec:failed-authentication-client-facing-eapol-analysis}

The failed client-facing capture corresponds to:

\begin{verbatim}
failed_8021x_ens38.pcap
\end{verbatim}

This capture was taken on \texttt{ens38}, the client-facing controlled segment between \texttt{client-1} and the Linux-based authenticator. In Wireshark, the failed capture shows a complete 802.1X / EAP exchange that progresses through the expected early stages but terminates unsuccessfully.

The visible sequence is:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item EAPOL Start
    \item EAP Request, Identity
    \item EAP Response, Identity
    \item EAP Request, MD5-Challenge
    \item EAP Response, MD5-Challenge
    \item EAP Failure
\end{enumerate}

This sequence is significant because it rules out several weaker explanations for the failure. The client did not fail due to lack of link, lack of EAPOL negotiation, or absence of authenticator response. The control-plane exchange clearly occurred and advanced into the MD5 challenge/response stage. The terminal outcome was explicitly \texttt{EAP Failure}.

Accordingly, the client-facing failed capture supports the same conclusion already reached through supplicant logs: the endpoint remained unauthorized because the authentication exchange completed with a negative result, not because the exchange never occurred.

\begin{figure}[H]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{assets/screenshots/Wireshark_failed_8021x_ens38_pcap.png}
    \caption{Failed wired 802.1X authentication on the client-facing EAPOL segment}
    \label{sec:fig-failed-8021x-ens38}
\end{figure}

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 10.5: FAILED AUTHENTICATION - AUTHENTICATOR-TO-RADIUS ANALYSIS
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection analyzes the failed upstream RADIUS capture and
% demonstrates that the backend AAA path completed normally through
% Access-Challenge and terminated explicitly with Access-Reject,
% confirming a negative authorization decision from FreeRADIUS.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{10.5 Failed Authentication: Authenticator-to-RADIUS Analysis}
\label{sec:failed-authentication-authenticator-to-radius-analysis}

The failed upstream RADIUS-side capture corresponds to:

\begin{verbatim}
failed_8021x_ens39.pcap
\end{verbatim}

This capture was taken on \texttt{ens39}, the routed side of \texttt{access-node} toward \texttt{dist-router} and the FreeRADIUS server. In Wireshark, the failed capture shows the corresponding backend AAA exchange that produced the client-facing failure observed on \texttt{ens38}.

The visible RADIUS transaction sequence is:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item Access-Request
    \item Access-Challenge
    \item Access-Request
    \item Access-Reject
\end{enumerate}

In the packet details for the terminal RADIUS response, the final \texttt{Access-Reject} carries an embedded EAP payload indicating \texttt{EAP Failure}. This is the strongest backend confirmation of the failed test case because it proves that the RADIUS server was reachable, the challenge-based workflow occurred normally, and the final authorization decision was explicitly negative.

This point matters analytically. It confirms that the failed incident was not caused by upstream transport loss or a silent AAA outage. The RADIUS service participated fully in the authentication exchange and returned an authoritative reject decision, which the client-facing side then reflected as \texttt{EAP Failure}.

\begin{figure}[H]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{assets/screenshots/Wireshark_failed_8021x_ens39_pcap.png}
    \caption{Failed wired 802.1X authentication on the authenticator-to-RADIUS path}
    \label{sec:fig-failed-8021x-ens39}
\end{figure}

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 10.6: SUCCESSFUL REMEDIATION RE-TEST - CLIENT-FACING EAPOL ANALYSIS
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection analyzes the successful post-remediation client-facing
% capture and establishes that the endpoint completed the same general
% IEEE 802.1X workflow as the failed case but terminated with EAP
% Success after credential restoration.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{10.6 Successful Remediation Re-Test: Client-Facing EAPOL Analysis}
\label{sec:successful-remediation-re-test-client-facing-eapol-analysis}

The successful client-facing remediation re-test capture corresponds to:

\begin{verbatim}
success_8021x_ens38.pcap
\end{verbatim}

This capture was also taken on \texttt{ens38}. In Wireshark, the successful capture shows the same general 802.1X / EAP structure as the failed case, but with a different terminal authorization result.

The visible sequence is:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item EAPOL Start
    \item EAP Request, Identity
    \item EAP Response, Identity
    \item EAP Request, MD5-Challenge
    \item EAP Response, MD5-Challenge
    \item EAP Success
\end{enumerate}

This is an important contrast with the failed capture. The early control-plane stages are substantially the same, which means the remediation did not alter the existence of the authentication workflow itself. Instead, it altered the final authorization result after the known-good credential state was restored.

The successful client-facing capture therefore confirms that the remediated endpoint completed the exchange and received an explicit \texttt{EAP Success}, which is the protocol-level counterpart to the authorized endpoint state observed in the supplicant and hostapd evidence.

\begin{figure}[H]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{assets/screenshots/Wireshark_success_8021x_ens38_pcap.png}
    \caption{Successful wired 802.1X authentication on the client-facing EAPOL segment}
    \label{sec:fig-success-8021x-ens38}
\end{figure}

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 10.7: SUCCESSFUL REMEDIATION RE-TEST - AUTHENTICATOR-TO-RADIUS ANALYSIS
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection analyzes the successful upstream RADIUS capture and
% demonstrates that the backend AAA workflow completed normally through
% Access-Challenge and terminated explicitly with Access-Accept,
% confirming restoration of successful authentication after remediation.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{10.7 Successful Remediation Re-Test: Authenticator-to-RADIUS Analysis}
\label{sec:successful-remediation-re-test-authenticator-to-radius-analysis}

The successful upstream RADIUS-side remediation re-test capture corresponds to:

\begin{verbatim}
success_8021x_ens39.pcap
\end{verbatim}

This capture was taken on \texttt{ens39}. In Wireshark, it shows the backend RADIUS exchange that corresponds to the successful client-facing authentication result.

The visible RADIUS transaction sequence is:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item Access-Request
    \item Access-Challenge
    \item Access-Request
    \item Access-Accept
\end{enumerate}

In the packet details for the terminal response, the \texttt{Access-Accept} contains an embedded EAP payload indicating \texttt{EAP Success}. This directly correlates with the client-facing \texttt{EAP Success} on \texttt{ens38} and provides authoritative AAA confirmation that the remediation restored successful backend authentication.

This is the protocol-level counterpart to the operational conclusion already established in the incident section: once the correct credential state was restored, the authentication workflow did not merely proceed farther or behave differently at random. It terminated with a positive authorization decision at the AAA layer.

\begin{figure}[H]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{assets/screenshots/Wireshark_success_8021x_ens39_pcap.png}
    \caption{Successful wired 802.1X authentication on the authenticator-to-RADIUS path}
    \label{sec:fig-success-8021x-ens39}
\end{figure}

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 10.8: COMPARATIVE PROTOCOL INTERPRETATION
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection compares the failed and successful capture sets and
% establishes that both tests followed the same general control-plane
% workflow but diverged only at the terminal authorization decision,
% which is the key analytical finding of the packet evidence.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{10.8 Comparative Protocol Interpretation}
\label{sec:comparative-protocol-interpretation}

The most important protocol distinction between the failed and successful capture sets is that both scenarios show a valid and complete 802.1X / EAP control-plane exchange through the Identity and MD5 challenge/response stages, but they terminate differently at both observation points.

In the failed case:
\begin{itemize}
    \item the client-facing \texttt{ens38} capture ends in \texttt{EAP Failure};
    \item the routed \texttt{ens39} capture ends in \texttt{Access-Reject}, carrying an embedded EAP failure indication.
\end{itemize}

In the successful remediation case:
\begin{itemize}
    \item the client-facing \texttt{ens38} capture ends in \texttt{EAP Success};
    \item the routed \texttt{ens39} capture ends in \texttt{Access-Accept}, carrying an embedded EAP success indication.
\end{itemize}

This is analytically important because it demonstrates that the failed test case was not caused by absence of EAPOL negotiation, lack of client participation, or general transport failure between the authenticator and FreeRADIUS. In both cases, the supplicant, authenticator, and AAA service all participated in a normal challenge-based authentication workflow. The decisive difference is the terminal policy result.

The captures therefore visually reinforce the distinction between the pre-authentication control-plane exchange and the final authorization outcome. Both failed and successful tests show substantially similar early negotiation behavior, but they diverge at the terminal decision point where authentication either remains unauthorized or transitions to authorized state.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 10.9: PUBLICATION AND REDACTION GUIDANCE
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection records the publication policy for packet
% screenshots and identifies the specific fields visible in the current
% Wireshark screenshots that must be redacted before public release.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{10.9 Publication and Redaction Guidance}
\label{sec:publication-and-redaction-guidance}

The publication policy for packet screenshots is:

\begin{itemize}
    \item private IP addresses may remain visible;
    \item MAC addresses must be redacted;
    \item passwords, shared secrets, and sensitive authentication blobs must be redacted;
    \item packet payload details that unnecessarily expose sensitive values should be redacted.
\end{itemize}

The following items should be redacted in the publication-safe versions:

\begin{itemize}
    \item all visible MAC addresses in the packet list pane;
    \item all visible MAC addresses in the packet details pane;
    \item MAC addresses exposed again in the hex pane;
    \item RADIUS \texttt{Authenticator} values where shown;
    \item \texttt{Message-Authenticator} values where shown;
\end{itemize}

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 10.10: SECTION CONCLUSION
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection closes the packet-analysis section by summarizing the
% evidentiary significance of the failed and successful capture pairs
% and restating why the protocol-layer validation materially strengthens
% the technical credibility of the lab.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{10.11 Section Conclusion}
\label{sec:section-10-conclusion}

The packet capture analysis materially strengthens the technical credibility of the lab. It shows that the incident and remediation were not validated solely through host logs, service states, or application-level reachability tests. They were also validated through deterministic protocol transitions at both the IEEE~802.1X and RADIUS layers.

The failed capture set demonstrates a complete challenge-based authentication workflow ending in \texttt{EAP Failure} and \texttt{Access-Reject}. The successful remediation capture set demonstrates the same workflow ending in \texttt{EAP Success} and \texttt{Access-Accept}. That contrast provides clean protocol-level confirmation that the simulated incident was a true authentication decision failure and that remediation restored normal authorization behavior.

The authoritative capture commands and packet inventory will be referenced again in the appendix material so that the packet-analysis section remains readable while still being fully supported by raw artifact traceability.

% =====================================================================
% SECTION 11: DESIGN CONSTRAINTS AND LIMITATIONS
% =====================================================================
% This section documents the intentional design boundaries of the lab so
% that the document accurately represents what was implemented, what was
% validated, and what was deliberately excluded. The purpose of the
% section is to prevent overstatement, preserve technical credibility,
% and distinguish the proof-of-concept objective from a claim of full
% enterprise production equivalence.
% =====================================================================

\section{11. Design Constraints and Limitations}
\label{sec:design-constraints-and-limitations}

The lab documented in this report is a deliberate proof-of-concept implementation intended to validate core NAC behaviors, incident handling workflow, and operational evidence collection in a constrained virtual environment. It is not presented as a one-to-one reproduction of a full enterprise campus access control deployment. This distinction is important and intentional.

The design succeeds at the objective for which it was built: it demonstrates credential-gated wired access control, protected and guest network segmentation, deterministic incident reproduction, structured remediation, lightweight automation, and packet-level protocol validation. However, it does so through a set of explicit design constraints chosen to keep the lab tractable, reproducible, and appropriate for portfolio documentation.

These limitations should not be interpreted as weaknesses in the proof-of-concept. On the contrary, making them explicit increases the credibility of the document because it clearly separates what was actually implemented and validated from what would exist in a larger or more production-like deployment.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 11.1: PLATFORM REALISM BOUNDARIES
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection defines the most important platform-level constraints
% in the lab, including the use of Linux hostapd as a functional wired
% authenticator approximation, the role of pfSense in guest isolation,
% and the treatment of virtualization-based segmentation as a lab
% implementation mechanism rather than a production architecture claim.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{11.1 Platform Realism Boundaries}
\label{sec:platform-realism-boundaries}

The most important platform constraint in the lab is that the wired 802.1X authenticator function is implemented using Linux \texttt{hostapd} in wired mode rather than a managed enterprise access switch. This design choice is fully appropriate for the proof-of-concept objective because it produces real IEEE~802.1X / EAPOL behavior and a real RADIUS-backed authorization workflow, but it should not be described as equivalent to a vendor campus switching platform.

In practical terms, the lab demonstrates credential-gated access control behavior rather than switch-native NAC feature breadth. It does not attempt to model enterprise access-switch capabilities such as dynamic VLAN assignment, downloadable ACLs, switch vendor posture integration, or centralized policy enforcement engines. The access decision in this lab is still meaningful and technically valid, but the implementation path is intentionally simplified.

Similarly, the guest network is enforced primarily through pfSense interface and rule policy rather than through dynamic 802.1X authorization state. This is an intentional architectural separation: the authenticated access path demonstrates wired 802.1X behavior, while the guest path demonstrates policy-based isolation and controlled internet access. Together they provide a credible functional access model without claiming to be a full enterprise NAC fabric.

Finally, the virtual network segmentation used throughout the lab should be understood as a lab isolation mechanism, not as a substitute for a production campus switching and security architecture. The VMware custom network design is useful because it provides deterministic path control and repeatable separation between segments, but it is not presented as equivalent to enterprise hardware switching, trunking, or controller-driven segmentation.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 11.2: AUTHENTICATION MODEL LIMITATIONS
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection documents the limitations of the chosen EAP method and
% explicitly distinguishes the lab's simplified EAP-MD5 demonstration
% from modern enterprise authentication practices such as certificate-
% based EAP methods.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{11.2 Authentication Model Limitations}
\label{sec:authentication-model-limitations}

The authentication workflow demonstrated in this lab is based on EAP-MD5 challenge/response, as confirmed both by the service logs and by the packet captures analyzed in Section~\ref{sec:packet-capture-analysis-tcpdump-and-wireshark}. This was an intentional choice made for demonstration simplicity and reproducibility in a small virtual lab.

That choice should not be interpreted as a modern production recommendation. EAP-MD5 is useful in this context because it provides a compact and easily observable challenge-based authentication flow that can be validated clearly in both logs and packet capture. It is especially useful for showing the exact distinction between a failed credential state and a successful post-remediation state. However, it does not provide the security properties expected of contemporary enterprise access control.

The lab does not demonstrate certificate-based EAP methods such as EAP-TLS, PEAP, or EAP-TTLS. It also does not attempt to model the certificate lifecycle, trust distribution, supplicant provisioning, or PKI operational dependencies that would normally accompany a more production-aligned 802.1X deployment. These omissions are intentional and should be understood as scope boundaries rather than gaps in execution.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 11.3: OBSERVABILITY AND SIEM LIMITATIONS
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection defines the boundary between the implemented logging
% and evidence-collection model and a true centralized SIEM deployment.
% It clarifies that Splunk Universal Forwarder was configured locally,
% while incident validation relied on multiple complementary evidence
% sources rather than centralized analytics.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{11.3 Observability and SIEM Limitations}
\label{sec:observability-and-siem-limitations}

The observability model in this lab is intentionally practical but limited in scope. As documented in Section~\ref{sec:observability-and-logging-pipeline}, Splunk is represented only by a Splunk Universal Forwarder with local monitored-input configuration. This is sufficient to demonstrate awareness of log source selection and forwarder-oriented ingestion configuration, but it is not a full Splunk search-head, indexer, or analytics deployment.

For that reason, the document does not claim centralized SIEM investigation capability. No centralized search workflow, correlation rules, dashboards, or alerting pipelines were demonstrated as part of the lab. Instead, incident validation relied on a combination of:

\begin{itemize}
    \item native service logs from \texttt{hostapd}, FreeRADIUS, and systemd/journald;
    \item manually captured debug artifacts preserved in the incident evidence directory;
    \item the GLPI incident record used as the operational ticketing system of record; and
    \item packet capture analysis using \texttt{tcpdump} and Wireshark.
\end{itemize}

This multi-source evidence model is entirely appropriate for the lab objective. It produces a defensible incident narrative and a strong audit trail without overstating the presence of centralized SIEM operations.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 11.4: AUTOMATION SCOPE LIMITATIONS
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection constrains the claims that can be made about the
% Python and Ansible automation used in the lab by explicitly defining
% them as purpose-built, validation-oriented, and tightly coupled to the
% known lab environment rather than generalized production frameworks.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{11.4 Automation Scope Limitations}
\label{sec:automation-scope-limitations}

The automation implemented in this lab is intentionally narrow in scope and should be described accordingly. As documented in Section~\ref{sec:automation-workflows}, the Ansible playbook is best characterized as a validation-first workflow with optional service remediation. It is designed to confirm service health, optionally restart key services, and preserve the resulting evidence locally. It is not intended to represent a full production change-management pipeline, orchestration framework, or large-scale infrastructure automation pattern.

The same principle applies to the Python parser. The parser is a purpose-built evidence summarizer that evaluates a known set of captured artifact files in fixed paths and produces a human-readable incident conclusion. It is valuable because it demonstrates structured evidence interpretation and repeatable operator validation, but it is not a generalized incident correlation engine or a reusable NAC analytics platform.

The tools are tightly aligned to the services, filenames, and operational checkpoints that were actually implemented and validated. The document should therefore present the automation as operationally useful and technically appropriate, while avoiding any implication that it is broader or more portable than it was designed to be.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 11.5: GUI AND EVIDENCE SCOPE LIMITATIONS
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection records the intentional decision to keep GUI evidence
% narrowly scoped to GLPI and Wireshark, and to rely primarily on
% command-line and protocol artifacts rather than broad administrative
% UI walkthroughs or hypervisor screenshots.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{11.5 GUI and Evidence Scope Limitations}
\label{sec:gui-and-evidence-scope-limitations}

The production document intentionally limits GUI evidence to two categories:

\begin{itemize}
    \item the GLPI incident record; and
    \item Wireshark packet-analysis screenshots.
\end{itemize}

This is an explicit documentation choice rather than an omission. The lab is primarily a command-line and protocol-driven implementation, and the strongest evidence is found in configuration files, service outputs, debug logs, packet traces, and deterministic test results. Broad GUI screenshot inventories would add visual bulk without materially improving the technical case.

For that reason, the document intentionally avoids dependence on hypervisor settings walkthroughs, appliance UI tours, or other screenshot-heavy administrative paths. The goal is to keep the evidence model focused on raw technical artifacts and protocol behavior while using GUI elements only where they provide uniquely valuable context, specifically:

\begin{itemize}
    \item GLPI as the human-readable incident management record; and
    \item Wireshark as the visual representation of protocol-layer success and failure.
\end{itemize}

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 11.6: OPERATIONAL SCOPE BOUNDARIES
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection defines the scale and operational limits of the lab,
% emphasizing that the implementation validates a single-endpoint NAC
% incident workflow and does not attempt to model redundancy, scale, or
% enterprise control-plane complexity beyond the proof-of-concept goal.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{11.6 Operational Scope Boundaries}
\label{sec:operational-scope-boundaries}

Operationally, the lab is centered on single-endpoint incident validation using \texttt{client-1} as the authoritative authenticated endpoint and \texttt{guest-test-1} as the guest-segment validation endpoint. This is sufficient for the design objective because the purpose of the lab is to prove that the access model, failure scenario, remediation, and evidence chain are all coherent and repeatable. It is not intended to simulate multi-endpoint concurrency or campus-scale endpoint churn.

Accordingly, the lab does not attempt to model any of the following:

\begin{itemize}
    \item redundant or load-balanced AAA infrastructure;
    \item high availability or failover behavior;
    \item large-scale endpoint inventory or onboarding workflows;
    \item enterprise certificate lifecycle and PKI operations;
    \item centralized NAC policy engines or posture frameworks;
    \item vendor switch telemetry, downloadable ACLs, or dynamic policy distribution.
\end{itemize}

These exclusions are intentional design boundaries. They do not diminish the proof-of-concept objective, which is to validate a coherent and technically defensible NAC incident workflow in a virtualized environment. In fact, by avoiding artificial breadth, the lab remains focused on a smaller set of claims that are strongly supported by direct evidence.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 11.7: CREDIBILITY THROUGH EXPLICIT CONSTRAINTS
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection explains why documenting limitations is itself a
% positive quality attribute of the report and frames the constraints as
% deliberate boundaries that improve accuracy and trustworthiness.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{11.7 Credibility Through Explicit Constraints}
\label{sec:credibility-through-explicit-constraints}

The constraints documented in this section should be understood as a strength of the report rather than as a disclaimer of weakness. In technical portfolio work, credibility is improved when the implementation is described precisely and bounded accurately. Overstating what a lab represents is more damaging than acknowledging where simplifications were made.

This lab makes strong, defensible claims in the areas where direct evidence exists:

\begin{itemize}
    \item functional wired 802.1X authentication behavior;
    \item RADIUS-backed authorization decisions;
    \item guest versus protected path differentiation;
    \item deterministic incident reproduction and remediation;
    \item operational evidence preservation through logs, automation, GLPI, and packet capture.
\end{itemize}

By explicitly stating what the lab does not attempt to represent, the document becomes more trustworthy. The result is a stronger portfolio artifact because the scope is narrow enough to be believable and broad enough to demonstrate meaningful engineering and operational skill.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 11.8: SECTION CONCLUSION
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection closes the limitations section by restating that the
% lab should be read as a disciplined proof-of-concept with intentional
% scope boundaries, not as a claim of full production equivalence.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{11.8 Section Conclusion}
\label{sec:section-11-conclusion}

This lab should be read as a disciplined proof-of-concept implementation with intentional scope boundaries. It successfully demonstrates the targeted behaviors it set out to validate: wired 802.1X access control, protected and guest path differentiation, incident reproduction, remediation, automation-assisted validation, and packet-level protocol corroboration.

It should not be interpreted as a full production NAC architecture or as a complete enterprise security platform. The design choices documented here were made deliberately to keep the lab reproducible, explainable, and strongly evidenced. Those constraints do not weaken the value of the implementation. They define it accurately.

The next section will use this bounded interpretation as the basis for a final conclusion and for a short discussion of logical future expansion paths that would extend the lab while preserving the same evidence-driven methodology.

% =====================================================================
% SECTION 12: CONCLUSION AND FUTURE EXPANSION OPPORTUNITIES
% =====================================================================
% This section concludes the document by synthesizing the technical
% outcomes of the lab, reaffirming the scope-bounded success criteria,
% and identifying realistic future expansion opportunities that would
% extend the proof-of-concept without overstating what was actually
% implemented in the current build.
% =====================================================================

\section{12. Conclusion and Future Expansion Opportunities}
\label{sec:conclusion-and-future-expansion-opportunities}

The lab documented in this report successfully met its original objective: to implement and validate a multi-segment network access control proof-of-concept that demonstrates wired 802.1X authentication, RADIUS-backed authorization, protected and guest path differentiation, incident simulation and remediation, lightweight automation, and packet-level protocol corroboration. Within the scope defined by the design, each major component was not only configured but also validated through direct operational evidence.

The authenticated path was shown to function through successful 802.1X supplicant behavior, hostapd authenticator processing, and FreeRADIUS acceptance. The guest path was shown to function through DHCP-based onboarding, gateway reachability, continued internet access, and enforced isolation from protected internal segments. The incident workflow further strengthened the implementation by proving that the environment could intentionally reproduce an authentication failure, preserve the resulting evidence, document the event in GLPI, restore correct configuration, and verify successful recovery. This moved the lab beyond simple service installation and into a more operationally credible validation model.

Equally important, the lab demonstrated end-to-end engineering continuity. The document does not merely show isolated services; it shows a coherent chain from design and segmentation through implementation, validation, troubleshooting, remediation, automation-assisted verification, and evidence preservation. That continuity is the strongest portfolio value of the project. It shows that the environment was built with enough rigor to support not only successful outcomes, but also controlled failure analysis and structured recovery.

As documented in Section~\ref{sec:design-constraints-and-limitations}, the lab is intentionally bounded and does not claim full enterprise production equivalence. That is a strength rather than a weakness. By explicitly limiting the claims to what was actually implemented and verified, the document remains technically credible and avoids overstating the realism of the environment. The result is a more trustworthy proof-of-concept and a stronger professional artifact.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 12.1: SUMMARY OF ACCOMPLISHMENTS
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection restates the major validated accomplishments of the
% lab in concise form so that the conclusion clearly ties the final
% assessment back to the core implementation and evidence sections.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{12.1 Summary of Accomplishments}
\label{sec:summary-of-accomplishments}

At a practical level, the lab achieved the following validated outcomes:

\begin{itemize}
    \item successful implementation of a multi-segment virtual network with distinct authenticated, guest, and service paths;
    \item successful wired 802.1X authentication flow using \texttt{wpa\_supplicant}, \texttt{hostapd} in wired mode, and FreeRADIUS;
    \item successful demonstration of guest-network DHCP assignment and enforced isolation from protected internal segments;
    \item successful reproduction of a realistic NAC-style authentication failure caused by incorrect endpoint credentials;
    \item successful remediation of that failure and confirmation of restored authorized access;
    \item successful documentation of the incident in GLPI as an operational record;
    \item successful implementation of lightweight Ansible-based service validation with optional remediation behavior; and
    \item successful packet-level corroboration of both failed and successful authentication states using \texttt{tcpdump} and Wireshark.
\end{itemize}

Taken together, these outcomes demonstrate that the lab was not merely assembled, but systematically tested and evidenced. That distinction is central to the value of the project.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 12.2: PROFESSIONAL AND PORTFOLIO VALUE
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection explains why the completed lab functions as a useful
% portfolio artifact by emphasizing the operational coherence, evidence-
% driven methodology, and disciplined scope control demonstrated across
% the report.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{12.2 Professional and Portfolio Value}
\label{sec:professional-and-portfolio-value}

The completed lab is a useful and credible portfolio piece because it demonstrates practical infrastructure and security-adjacent engineering in a disciplined way. It shows that the environment was designed intentionally, implemented from first principles, validated with repeatable test procedures, and documented with a strong evidentiary chain.

From a professional standpoint, the lab is especially valuable because it combines multiple modes of technical work in one coherent project:

\begin{itemize}
    \item network segmentation and path design;
    \item AAA and access-control service configuration;
    \item endpoint supplicant testing;
    \item troubleshooting and incident-style reasoning;
    \item log and packet evidence interpretation;
    \item automation-assisted operational validation; and
    \item structured technical documentation.
\end{itemize}

This breadth is meaningful because it reflects how real infrastructure work is often evaluated: not by whether a single service starts, but by whether the operator can connect architecture, implementation, validation, and evidence into a coherent operational story. This document does that successfully.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 12.3: FUTURE EXPANSION OPPORTUNITIES
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection identifies realistic next-step enhancements that would
% extend the lab while remaining clearly framed as future work rather
% than as features already implemented in the current environment.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{12.3 Future Expansion Opportunities}
\label{sec:future-expansion-opportunities}

Although the current implementation is complete within its stated scope, it also provides a strong foundation for future expansion. Several next-step enhancements would extend the realism or operational depth of the lab while preserving the same evidence-driven methodology used throughout this report.

A natural first expansion would be to replace the current EAP-MD5 demonstration method with a more production-aligned EAP model such as EAP-TLS, PEAP, or EAP-TTLS. This would significantly increase realism by introducing stronger authentication semantics and a more modern enterprise access-control pattern, while also creating opportunities to document certificate-based supplicant behavior and more advanced troubleshooting.

A second useful expansion would be to explore more dynamic authorization behavior. Within the constraints of a virtual lab, this could include experiments that more closely approximate switch-driven policy changes, segmented access outcomes, or more granular authorization handling beyond the current binary authenticated-versus-guest distinction. The goal would not be to claim full enterprise feature parity, but to extend the lab toward richer policy demonstrations.

A third expansion path would be to deepen observability. Section~\ref{sec:observability-and-logging-pipeline} intentionally documents a local Splunk Universal Forwarder monitored-input configuration rather than a full centralized analytics stack. A future iteration could extend this into a more complete logging pipeline with centralized indexing, search, and event correlation, thereby improving the analytical side of the incident workflow.

The automation layer also provides room for meaningful growth. The current Ansible playbook and Python parser are intentionally narrow, which is appropriate for the present proof-of-concept. Future versions could add richer health checks, more structured output formats, broader artifact collection logic, or more generalized remediation patterns while still preserving the validation-first operating model established in Section~\ref{sec:automation-workflows}.

Scale realism could also be improved by introducing multiple authenticated endpoints or additional controlled failure scenarios. The current single-endpoint incident workflow is sufficient to prove the concept, but repeated validation across multiple clients or multiple failure classes would strengthen the repeatability argument and provide more varied operational evidence.

Finally, the GLPI workflow could be extended from its current manual but effective incident-record role into a more formalized evidence-management process. Future iterations could attach or reference more structured artifacts directly, impose a more consistent incident template, or align the ticketing workflow more closely with the operational outputs generated by the automation layer.

Each of these expansion opportunities is intentionally framed as future work. None of them are necessary to validate the success of the current build. Rather, they demonstrate that the lab has been constructed on a foundation strong enough to support meaningful iterative growth.

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION 12.4: FINAL ASSESSMENT
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection provides the final evaluative judgment of the lab in
% concise form, closing the report with a scope-bounded but affirmative
% statement of technical success and professional usefulness.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{12.4 Final Assessment}
\label{sec:final-assessment}

In final assessment, the lab should be considered successful both as a technical implementation and as a professional documentation artifact. It achieved the intended proof-of-concept objectives, produced direct evidence for each major claim, and maintained strong internal consistency between architecture, validation, incident handling, automation, and protocol analysis.

Just as importantly, the document avoids the common failure mode of overstatement. It does not claim enterprise completeness where none was implemented. Instead, it presents a bounded, defensible, and well-evidenced engineering exercise that demonstrates practical competence across several adjacent domains.

Within its stated scope, the lab provides a technically defensible and well-documented proof-of-concept for wired NAC validation.

\subsection{A.2 Authenticator, Supplicant, and AAA Configurations}
\label{sec:appendix-a-authenticator-supplicant-and-aaa-configurations}

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION A.2.1: HOSTAPD WIRED CONFIGURATION
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection preserves the final wired-mode hostapd configuration
% used by access-node to act as the lab authenticator for client-1.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsubsection{A.2.1 hostapd Wired Configuration}
\label{sec:appendix-a-access-node-hostapd-wired-config}

\begin{verbatim}
access-node: /etc/hostapd/lab/hostapd-wired.conf
interface=ens38
driver=wired

ieee8021x=1
eapol_version=2
eap_reauth_period=3600

auth_server_addr=10.0.30.10
auth_server_port=1812
auth_server_shared_secret=<REDACTED>

own_ip_addr=10.0.23.1

logger_stdout=-1
logger_stdout_level=0
logger_syslog=-1
logger_syslog_level=0

ctrl_interface=/var/run/hostapd
ctrl_interface_group=0
\end{verbatim}

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION A.2.2: HOSTAPD WIRED SYSTEMD UNIT
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection preserves the systemd unit used to run hostapd in
% wired 802.1X mode on access-node.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsubsection{A.2.2 hostapd Wired systemd Unit}
\label{sec:appendix-a-access-node-hostapd-wired-systemd-unit}

\begin{verbatim}
access-node: /etc/systemd/system/hostapd-wired.service
[Unit]
Description=hostapd wired 802.1X authenticator (lab)
After=network-online.target
Wants=network-online.target

[Service]
Type=simple
ExecStart=/usr/sbin/hostapd -dd /etc/hostapd/lab/hostapd-wired.conf
Restart=on-failure
RestartSec=2

[Install]
WantedBy=multi-user.target
\end{verbatim}

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION A.2.3: CLIENT-1 WPA_SUPPLICANT WIRED CONFIGURATION
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection preserves the final wired supplicant configuration
% used by client-1 during successful authenticated operation.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsubsection{A.2.3 client-1 wpa\_supplicant Wired Configuration}
\label{sec:appendix-a-client-1-wpa-supplicant-wired-8021x-config}

\begin{verbatim}
client-1: /etc/wpa_supplicant/lab/wired-8021x.conf
ctrl_interface=/run/wpa_supplicant
ap_scan=0
eapol_version=2

network={
    key_mgmt=IEEE8021X
    eap=MD5
    identity="nacuser"
    password="<REDACTED>"
}
\end{verbatim}

\subsubsection{A.2.4 FreeRADIUS Clients Configuration}
\label{sec:appendix-a-radius-splunk-freeradius-clients-config}

\begin{verbatim}
radius-splunk: /etc/freeradius/3.0/clients.conf
# -*- text -*-
##
## clients.conf -- client configuration directives
##
##	$Id: <REDACTED> $

#######################################################################
#
#  Define RADIUS clients (usually a NAS, Access Point, etc.).
#
#  [commentary omitted in summary: file preserved exactly as provided]
#
client localhost {
	ipaddr = 127.0.0.1
	proto = *
	secret = testing123
	nas_type = other
	limit {
		max_connections = 16
		lifetime = 0
		idle_timeout = 30
	}
}

client localhost_ipv6 {
	ipv6addr	= ::1
	secret		= testing123
}

client access-node {
    ipaddr = 10.0.23.1
    secret = <REDACTED>
    shortname = access-node
}
\end{verbatim}

	Framed-Protocol = SLIP

Enabled (Enable DHCP server on GUEST interface)
Subnet: 10.0.20.0/24
Subnet Range: 10.0.20.1 - 10.0.20.254
Address Pool Range: 10.0.20.100 - 10.0.20.199
\end{verbatim}

\subsubsection{A.3.3 pfSense Guest Firewall Rules}
\label{sec:appendix-a-pfsense-guest-firewall-rules}

\begin{verbatim}
Pass  | IPv4* | Source: 10.0.20.0/24 | Destination: This Firewall (Self) 
        | ALLOW-GUEST-TO-PFSENSE
Block | IPv4* | Source: 10.0.20.0/24 | Destination: 10.0.30.0/24       
        | BLOCK-GUEST-TO-SERVICES
Block | IPv4* | Source: 10.0.20.0/24 | Destination: 10.0.34.0/30       
        | BLOCK-GUEST-TO-TRANSIT
Block | IPv4* | Source: 10.0.20.0/24 | Destination: 10.0.23.0/24       
        | BLOCK-GUEST-TO-DIST
Block | IPv4* | Source: 10.0.20.0/24 | Destination: 10.0.12.0/24       
        | BLOCK-GUEST-TO-AUTH-ACCESS
Pass  | IPv4* | Source: 10.0.20.0/24 | Destination: *                  
        | ALLOW-GUEST-TO-INTERNET-AND-OTHER-NON-INTERNAL
\end{verbatim}

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION A.4.2: ANSIBLE NAC VALIDATION PLAYBOOK
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection preserves the final authoritative Ansible playbook
% used for service validation, optional remediation, and local artifact
% capture.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsubsection{A.4.2 Ansible NAC Validation Playbook}
\label{sec:appendix-a-ansible-nac-validation-playbook}

\begin{verbatim}
radius-splunk: ~/ansible-nac/playbooks/nac_validation.yml
---
- name: Validate NAC services and optionally remediate
  hosts: nac_nodes
  gather_facts: false
  become: false

  vars:
    remediate: false
    artifact_dir: "/home/nac-lab/ansible-nac/artifacts"

  tasks:
    - name: Collect basic hostname
      command: hostname
      register: hostname_out
      changed_when: false

    - name: Check hostapd-wired service on access-node
      become: true
      command: systemctl status hostapd-wired.service --no-pager
      register: hostapd_status_before
      changed_when: false
      failed_when: false
      when: inventory_hostname == "access-node"

    - name: Check freeradius service on radius-splunk
      become: true
      command: systemctl status freeradius --no-pager
      register: freeradius_status_before
      changed_when: false
      failed_when: false
      when: inventory_hostname == "radius-splunk"

    - name: Restart hostapd-wired service when remediation is enabled
      become: true
      command: systemctl restart hostapd-wired.service
      register: hostapd_restart
      changed_when: true
      failed_when: false
      when:
        - inventory_hostname == "access-node"
        - remediate | bool

    - name: Restart freeradius service when remediation is enabled
      become: true
      command: systemctl restart freeradius
      register: freeradius_restart
      changed_when: true
      failed_when: false
      when:
        - inventory_hostname == "radius-splunk"
        - remediate | bool

    - name: Re-check hostapd-wired service on access-node
      become: true
      command: systemctl status hostapd-wired.service --no-pager
      register: hostapd_status_after
      changed_when: false
      failed_when: false
      when: inventory_hostname == "access-node"

    - name: Re-check freeradius service on radius-splunk
      become: true
      command: systemctl status freeradius --no-pager
      register: freeradius_status_after
      changed_when: false
      failed_when: false
      when: inventory_hostname == "radius-splunk"

    - name: Collect recent hostapd logs
      become: true
      command: journalctl -u hostapd-wired.service -n 40 --no-pager
      register: hostapd_logs
      changed_when: false
      failed_when: false
      when: inventory_hostname == "access-node"

    - name: Collect recent freeradius service logs
      become: true
      command: journalctl -u freeradius -n 40 --no-pager
      register: freeradius_logs
      changed_when: false
      failed_when: false
      when: inventory_hostname == "radius-splunk"

    - name: Collect recent freeradius application log
      become: true
      command: tail -n 40 /var/log/freeradius/radius.log
      register: freeradius_radiuslog
      changed_when: false
      failed_when: false
      when: inventory_hostname == "radius-splunk"

    - name: Save hostapd pre-check status locally
      become: false
      delegate_to: localhost
      copy:
        content: "{{ hostapd_status_before.stdout | default('N/A') }}"
        dest: "{{ artifact_dir }}/{{ inventory_hostname }}_hostapd_status_before.txt"
      when: inventory_hostname == "access-node"

    - name: Save hostapd post-check status locally
      become: false
      delegate_to: localhost
      copy:
        content: "{{ hostapd_status_after.stdout | default('N/A') }}"
        dest: "{{ artifact_dir }}/{{ inventory_hostname }}_hostapd_status_after.txt"
      when: inventory_hostname == "access-node"

    - name: Save hostapd restart result locally
      become: false
      delegate_to: localhost
      copy:
        content: "{{ hostapd_restart.stdout | default('remediation not requested') }}"
        dest: "{{ artifact_dir }}/{{ inventory_hostname }}_hostapd_restart_result.txt"
      when: inventory_hostname == "access-node"

    - name: Save hostapd logs locally
      become: false
      delegate_to: localhost
      copy:
        content: "{{ hostapd_logs.stdout | default('N/A') }}"
        dest: "{{ artifact_dir }}/{{ inventory_hostname }}_hostapd_logs.txt"
      when: inventory_hostname == "access-node"

    - name: Save freeradius pre-check status locally
      become: false
      delegate_to: localhost
      copy:
        content: "{{ freeradius_status_before.stdout | default('N/A') }}"
        dest: "{{ artifact_dir }}/{{ inventory_hostname }}_freeradius_status_before.txt"
      when: inventory_hostname == "radius-splunk"

    - name: Save freeradius post-check status locally
      become: false
      delegate_to: localhost
      copy:
        content: "{{ freeradius_status_after.stdout | default('N/A') }}"
        dest: "{{ artifact_dir }}/{{ inventory_hostname }}_freeradius_status_after.txt"
      when: inventory_hostname == "radius-splunk"

    - name: Save freeradius restart result locally
      become: false
      delegate_to: localhost
      copy:
        content: "{{ freeradius_restart.stdout | default('remediation not requested') }}"
        dest: "{{ artifact_dir }}/{{ inventory_hostname }}_freeradius_restart_result.txt"
      when: inventory_hostname == "radius-splunk"

    - name: Save freeradius service logs locally
      become: false
      delegate_to: localhost
      copy:
        content: "{{ freeradius_logs.stdout | default('N/A') }}"
        dest: "{{ artifact_dir }}/{{ inventory_hostname }}_freeradius_journal.txt"
      when: inventory_hostname == "radius-splunk"

    - name: Save freeradius application log locally
      become: false
      delegate_to: localhost
      copy:
        content: "{{ freeradius_radiuslog.stdout | default('N/A') }}"
        dest: "{{ artifact_dir }}/{{ inventory_hostname }}_freeradius_radiuslog.txt"
      when: inventory_hostname == "radius-splunk"
\end{verbatim}

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION A.4.3: PYTHON NAC INCIDENT PARSER
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection preserves the final Python parser used to summarize
% the incident evidence set.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsubsection{A.4.3 Python NAC Incident Parser}
\label{sec:appendix-a-python-nac-incident-parser}

\begin{verbatim}
radius-splunk: ~/incident-evidence/nac_incident_parser.py
#!/usr/bin/env python3

from pathlib import Path

BASE = Path.home() / "incident-evidence"

FILES = {
    "client_failed": BASE / "wpa_supplicant_failed_auth_incident.txt",
    "client_success": BASE / "wpa_supplicant_success_retest_v2.txt",
    "hostapd_failed": BASE / "hostapd_failed_auth_incident.txt",
    "hostapd_success": BASE / "hostapd_success_retest_v2.txt",
    "radius_failed": BASE / "freeradius_failed_auth_incident.txt",
}

def read_text(path):
    try:
        return path.read_text(errors="ignore")
    except FileNotFoundError:
        return None

def present(text, needles):
    if text is None:
        return False
    return any(n in text for n in needles)

def summarize():
    data = {name: read_text(path) for name, path in FILES.items()}

    results = {
        "client_failed_unauthorized": present(data["client_failed"], [
            "Supplicant port status: Unauthorized"
        ]),
        "client_failed_connected": present(data["client_failed"], [
            "CTRL-EVENT-CONNECTED"
        ]),
        "client_success_connected": present(data["client_success"], [
            "CTRL-EVENT-CONNECTED"
        ]),
        "client_success_authorized": present(data["client_success"], [
            "Supplicant port status: Authorized"
        ]),
        "hostapd_failed_attempt_seen": present(data["hostapd_failed"], [
            "IEEE 802.1X: start authentication",
            "RADIUS Sending RADIUS message to authentication server",
            "CTRL-EVENT-EAP-STARTED",
            "recvmsg [RADIUS]"
        ]),
        "hostapd_success_authorized": present(data["hostapd_success"], [
            "AP-STA-CONNECTED",
            "authorizing port",
            "CTRL-EVENT-EAP-SUCCESS"
        ]),
        "radius_failed_reject": present(data["radius_failed"], [
            "Failed to authenticate the user",
            "Using Post-Auth-Type Reject",
            "EAP Failure",
            "Sending EAP Failure"
        ]),
    }

    print("=== NAC Incident Evidence Summary ===")
    print()

    print("[1] Failed authentication simulation")
    print(f"- Client remained unauthorized: {'YES' if results['client_failed_unauthorized']
            else 'NO'}")
    print(f"- Client showed successful connection during failed test: {'YES' if 
            results['client_failed_connected'] else 'NO'}")
    print(f"- hostapd saw authentication attempt: {'YES' if 
            results['hostapd_failed_attempt_seen'] else 'NO'}")
    print(f"- FreeRADIUS recorded failed auth / reject path: {'YES' if 
            results['radius_failed_reject'] else 'NO'}")
    print()

    print("[2] Remediation / successful re-test")
    print(f"- Client reached CTRL-EVENT-CONNECTED: {'YES' if 
            results['client_success_connected'] else 'NO'}")
    print(f"- Client reached Authorized state: {'YES' if 
            results['client_success_authorized'] else 'NO'}")
    print(f"- hostapd showed successful authorization indicators: {'YES' if 
            results['hostapd_success_authorized'] else 'NO'}")
    print()

    if (
        results["client_failed_unauthorized"]
        and results["radius_failed_reject"]
        and results["client_success_connected"]
        and results["client_success_authorized"]
        and results["hostapd_success_authorized"]
    ):
        print("[3] Incident conclusion")
        print("- SIMULATED NAC INCIDENT SUCCESSFULLY REPRODUCED AND REMEDIATED")
    else:
        print("[3] Incident conclusion")
        print("- Evidence is incomplete or mixed; review raw logs for additional detail")

if __name__ == "__main__":
    summarize()
\end{verbatim}

ens33: Disconnect event - remove keys
ens33: State: ASSOCIATED -> DISCONNECTED
EAPOL: External notification - portEnabled=0
EAPOL: SUPP_PAE entering state DISCONNECTED
EAPOL: Supplicant port status: Unauthorized
EAPOL: SUPP_BE entering state INITIALIZE
EAP: EAP entering state DISABLED
EAPOL: External notification - portValid=0
ens33: State: DISCONNECTED -> DISCONNECTED
EAPOL: External notification - portEnabled=0
EAPOL: External notification - portValid=0
QM: Clear all active DSCP policies
ens33: CTRL-EVENT-DSCP-POLICY clear_all
EAP: deinitialize previously used EAP method (4, MD5) at EAP deinit
ens33: WPA: Clear old PMK and PTK
ens33: Cancelling scan request
ens33: Cancelling authentication timeout
Off-channel: Clear pending Action frame TX (pending_action_tx=(nil)
HS20: Delete all stored icons
Off-channel: Action frame sequence done notification: pending_action_tx=(nil) drv_offchan_
        tx=0 action_tx_wait_time=0 off_channel_freq=0 roc_waiting_drv_freq=0
QM: Clear all active DSCP policies
ens33: CTRL-EVENT-DSCP-POLICY clear_all
Remove interface ens33 from radio 
Remove radio 
ens33: CTRL-EVENT-TERMINATING 
\end{verbatim}

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION B.1.6: NAC INCIDENT PARSER SUCCESS OUTPUT
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection preserves the parser output file demonstrating that
% the collected incident evidence was successfully summarized into a
% deterministic incident conclusion.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsubsection{B.1.6 NAC Incident Parser Success Output}
\label{sec:appendix-b-nac-incident-parser-success-output}

\begin{verbatim}
radius-splunk: ~/incident-evidence/nac_incident_parser_success.txt
=== NAC Incident Evidence Summary ===

[1] Failed authentication simulation
- Client remained unauthorized: YES
- Client showed successful connection during failed test: NO
- hostapd saw authentication attempt: YES
- FreeRADIUS recorded failed auth / reject path: YES

[2] Remediation / successful re-test
- Client reached CTRL-EVENT-CONNECTED: YES
- Client reached Authorized state: YES
- hostapd showed successful authorization indicators: YES

[3] Incident conclusion
- SIMULATED NAC INCIDENT SUCCESSFULLY REPRODUCED AND REMEDIATED
\end{verbatim}

% referenced in the body of the report.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsection{B.2 GLPI Incident Record}
\label{sec:appendix-b-glpi-incident-record}

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION B.2.1: GLPI INCIDENT TITLE
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection preserves the exact title of the GLPI incident ticket
% created to document the wired 802.1X failure and remediation event.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsubsection{B.2.1 GLPI Incident Title}
\label{sec:appendix-b-glpi-incident-title}

\begin{verbatim}
Wired 802.1X authentication failure and remediation for client-1
\end{verbatim}

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION B.2.2: GLPI INCIDENT STATUS
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection preserves the final status of the GLPI incident
% ticket after remediation and validation were completed.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsubsection{B.2.2 GLPI Incident Status}
\label{sec:appendix-b-glpi-incident-status}

\begin{verbatim}
Solved
\end{verbatim}

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION B.2.3: GLPI INCIDENT RECORD RAW TEXT
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection preserves the full incident text entered into GLPI so
% that the GUI record referenced in the body remains textually
% reproducible in the appendix.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsubsection{B.2.3 GLPI Incident Record Raw Text}
\label{sec:appendix-b-glpi-incident-record-raw-text}

\begin{verbatim}
Incident summary:
client-1 failed wired 802.1X authentication during NAC validation after
the supplicant password was intentionally changed to an incorrect value
to simulate a credential issue.

Observed behavior:
- client-1 remained unauthorized during the failed test
- FreeRADIUS showed a failed authentication / reject path
- hostapd on access-node recorded the 802.1X authentication attempt

Investigation:
- Verified FreeRADIUS service health on radius-splunk
- Confirmed the known-good credentials still produced Access-Accept
  using radtest
- Revalidated the client wpa_supplicant configuration

Remediation:
- Restored the known-good wpa_supplicant credentials on client-1
- Re-ran the wired 802.1X authentication test

Recovery validation:
- client-1 showed CTRL-EVENT-CONNECTED
- client-1 supplicant port reached Authorized state
- access-node hostapd logs showed AP-STA-CONNECTED and port
  authorization

Impact:
This simulated a realistic enterprise NAC incident involving endpoint
credential failure and demonstrated successful troubleshooting and
service restoration.
\end{verbatim}

TASK [Collect basic hostname] **************************************************
ok: [radius-splunk]
ok: [access-node]

TASK [Check hostapd-wired service on access-node] ******************************
skipping: [radius-splunk]
ok: [access-node]

skipping: [access-node]
ok: [radius-splunk]

skipping: [radius-splunk]
skipping: [access-node]

skipping: [access-node]
skipping: [radius-splunk]

TASK [Re-check hostapd-wired service on access-node] ***************************
skipping: [radius-splunk]
ok: [access-node]

TASK [Re-check freeradius service on radius-splunk] ****************************
skipping: [access-node]
ok: [radius-splunk]

TASK [Collect recent hostapd logs] *********************************************
skipping: [radius-splunk]
ok: [access-node]

skipping: [access-node]
ok: [radius-splunk]

ok: [radius-splunk]

skipping: [radius-splunk]
changed: [access-node -> localhost]

TASK [Save hostapd post-check status locally] **********************************
skipping: [radius-splunk]
changed: [access-node -> localhost]

TASK [Save hostapd restart result locally] *************************************
skipping: [radius-splunk]

% Wireshark figure mapping, and the data redaction policy.

\subsection{B.6 Packet Capture Metadata and Figure Mapping}
\label{sec:appendix-b-packet-capture-metadata-and-figure-mapping}

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION B.6.1: PCAP FILE INVENTORY
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection preserves the authoritative pcap inventory listing
% referenced in the packet-analysis section.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsubsection{B.6.1 pcap File Inventory}
\label{sec:appendix-b-pcap-file-inventory}

\begin{verbatim}
access-node: ls -lh ~/pcaps
-rw-r--r-- 1 nac-lab nac-lab 385 Mar 23 20:23 failed_8021x_ens38.pcap
-rw-r--r-- 1 nac-lab nac-lab 738 Mar 23 20:23 failed_8021x_ens39.pcap
-rw-r--r-- 1 nac-lab nac-lab 385 Mar 23 20:29 success_8021x_ens38.pcap
-rw-r--r-- 1 nac-lab nac-lab 747 Mar 23 20:29 success_8021x_ens39.pcap
\end{verbatim}

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION B.6.2: PACKET CAPTURE METHODOLOGY AND TCPDUMP COMMANDS
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection preserves the authoritative packet-capture commands
% used to produce the failed and successful authentication pcaps.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsubsection{B.6.2 Packet Capture Methodology and tcpdump Commands}
\label{sec:appendix-b-packet-capture-methodology-and-tcpdump-commands}

\begin{verbatim}
access-node:
sudo timeout 20s tcpdump -i ens38 -w /tmp/failed_8021x_ens38.pcap ether proto 0x888e &
sudo timeout 20s tcpdump -i ens39 -w /tmp/failed_8021x_ens39.pcap host 10.0.30.10 and 
        udp port 1812 &
sleep 2

client-1:
sudo systemctl stop wpa_supplicant
sudo timeout 15s wpa_supplicant -i ens33 -D wired -c 
        /etc/wpa_supplicant/lab/wired-8021x.conf -dd

access-node:
sudo timeout 20s tcpdump -i ens38 -w ~/pcaps/success_8021x_ens38.pcap ether proto 0x888e &
sudo timeout 20s tcpdump -i ens39 -w ~/pcaps/success_8021x_ens39.pcap host 10.0.30.10 and
        udp port 1812 &
sleep 2

client-1:
sudo systemctl stop wpa_supplicant
sudo timeout 15s wpa_supplicant -i ens33 -D wired -c 
        /etc/wpa_supplicant/lab/wired-8021x.conf -dd
\end{verbatim}

% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% SUBSECTION B.6.3: PACKET CAPTURE REDACTION POLICY
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------
% This subsection preserves the publication-safe redaction
% policy for Wireshark packet screenshots.
% ---------------------------------------------------------------------

\subsubsection{B.6.4 Packet Capture Redaction Policy}
\label{sec:appendix-b-packet-capture-redaction-policy}

\begin{verbatim}
Publication policy:
- private IP addresses: keep visible
- MAC addresses: redact
- passwords/shared secrets/auth blobs: redact
- packet payload details that expose secrets: redact

Accordingly, the publication-safe Wireshark images should redact:
- MAC addresses in the packet list pane
- MAC addresses in the packet details pane
- MAC addresses in the hex pane
- RADIUS Authenticator values where shown
- Message-Authenticator values where shown
\end{verbatim}
